Here's what I found:\n\n## Key Visual Characteristics of Rubber Hose Animation\n\nRubber hose animation is named for the fluid, highly elastic movement of characters' limbs, resembling flexible rubber hoses. Character designs are significantly simplified, typically featuring round faces and bodies with minimal anatomical detail, while limbs are often depicted as thin, elastic tubes, allowing for highly fluid and exaggerated movements.\n\nEyes are commonly large and round, frequently rendered in a \"pie-eyed\" style, giving characters a wide-eyed, often innocent, appearance, while iconic accessories like white gloves and oversized big shoes are also prevalent features of the character designs.\n\n## Fleischer Studios' Role\n\nThe Fleischer Style is an iconic animation aesthetic that rose to prominence primarily in the 1920s and 1930s, largely developed by Fleischer Studios. Fleischer Studios held on to the rubber-hose style the longest, finally conforming to the more contemporary West Coast animation style by 1940.\n\nAnimation studios such as the Fleischer Brothers embraced the elastic, surreal movement that defined the era, producing inventive shorts like Out of the Inkwell.\n\n## Visual Elements and Design Principles\n\nThis animation style prioritizes expressive motion over anatomical accuracy. These cartoons share a visual identity - think heavy use of black inks on white backgrounds and exaggerated facial expressions, with flailing rubber-hose limbs without joints.\n\nThis search provides comprehensive information about the rubber hose animation style's characteristics, its prominence in 1930s cartoons, and Fleischer Studios' significant role in developing and maintaining this influential aesthetic.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"4d5de240-b522-4643-8763-feb7554008dc","timestamp":"2026-04-06T14:53:03.852Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"6b129e2d-3afb-4807-8513-26610c59e8d6","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/root","sessionId":"00431266-ecaa-4922-87fe-ffcae2b0b919","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"main","slug":"misty-purring-bentley"}

Here are the key findings:\n\n## Squash and Stretch Animation Principle\n\nSquash and Stretch (S&S) is the principle of applying a contrasting change of shape—from a squash pose to a stretch pose or vice versa—to give a feeling of fleshiness, flexibility, and life in animation. When an object impacts, it should squash down. When it moves quickly, it should stretch.\n\n## Smear Frames Technique\n\nDistorting a frame can be helpful to show fast movements in animation. Distorted frames are often called smear frames, and they are usually hand drawn frames that can act similar to motion blur when implemented properly. Smear frames bridge the gap between individual frames, contributing significantly to the illusion of smooth, lifelike movement. These subtle, elongated, or stretched frames create a sense of speed, impact, and energy, enhancing the overall visual experience.\n\n## AI Video Generation Prompting\n\nThe search revealed that in AI video generation prompting, smear frames can be specified as a visual element—for example, \"only black speed lines, impact rings, smear frames, skid marks, and sharp red blood accents.\" This shows how animation principles like smear frames are being incorporated into structured prompts for AI video generation tools.\n\n## Complementary Techniques\n\nSmear frames are most powerful when used alongside other animation techniques like squash and stretch, anticipation, and motion blur.\n\nThe search results show how these classical animation principles are being adapted for modern AI video generation workflows through detailed prompt engineering.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"7d7f2d59-0d30-4c61-ab09-19162f9e3634","timestamp":"2026-04-06T14:53:15.023Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"983001a6-529c-41e3-b3fd-b3990b9416d0","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/root","sessionId":"00431266-ecaa-4922-87fe-ffcae2b0b919","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"main","slug":"misty-purring-bentley"}

This principle is used prevalently in old Looney Tunes cartoon characters, and the greater the action for anticipation in animation, the more cartoony and comedic it can look. Here's what I discovered:\n\n## Line Weight and Outline Conventions\n\nThick-line animation is characterized by visible heavy black borders around characters and objects. Thicker lines for the outline help your character stand out against the background. This technique has specific design purposes: thinner lines for features like facial expressions or clothing folds offer a subtle touch without overpowering the overall image.\n\nBy varying line thickness, you can create an illusion of depth, with thicker lines used for objects closer to the viewer and thinner ones for those further away.\n\n## Cel Animation and Visual Design\n\nThe outline of the images are drawn on the front of the cel while colors are painted on the back to eliminate brushstrokes. Conventional smooth lighting values are calculated for each pixel and then quantized to a small number of discrete shades to create the characteristic \"flat look\", where the shadows and highlights appear as blocks of color rather than being smoothly mixed in a gradient.\n\n## Bold Outlines in Modern Animation\n\nStrong black lines frame objects and characters clearly in cel-shaded art, and these bold outlines make shapes stand out well from backgrounds and add a comic feel. Outline detection is key in accomplishing the dramatic, cartoonish look of cel-shaded animations by outlining edges with heavy, black lines to isolate objects and characters from the backdrop.\n\n## Flat Color and Limited Palette Conventions\n\nThe process uses solid, even colors without blending or smooth gradients, which keeps the style simple and emphasizes the shaded effect clearly. Characters drawn in this style have thin/noodley or fat arms, rounded or bean-shaped heads, bean-shaped mouths, letter-shaped noses, and big or tiny eyes.\n\nThe rounded character models and reduced detail give characters an iconographic look where they remain recognizable even in silhouette, featuring noodle arms, nondescript dot and line mouths and eyes, stubbed hands, and basic attire with few adornments.\n\n## Technical and Practical Reasons\n\nThin-line animation has a lot to do with the shift from pencil and paper to digital animation, as drawing with thin lines makes the animation process easier and faster in Flash and other computer programs. The need for quicker, cheaper animation after the economic downturn may also have driven the desire for more cheap, yet still pleasing animation styles.\n\n## Criticism and Controversy\n\nThe term \"CalArts\" style is used to, more often than not, criticize the simpler character designs of many shows. One of the main criticisms is that it shows laziness from cartoon creators, and many complain that the style is too simplistic and brings less diversity to design and animation.\n\nHowever, while some creators like Daron Nefcy (Star vs the Forces of Evil) and Alex Hirsch (Gravity Falls) attended CalArts, others came from different schools, suggesting that CalArts style doesn't exist in the way many people think it does.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"0b6938cf-73ac-4491-abc1-0a063f41a0ae","timestamp":"2026-04-06T14:55:48.578Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"65edead1-347c-485e-82f7-7eafc1b5a214","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/root","sessionId":"00431266-ecaa-4922-87fe-ffcae2b0b919","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"main","slug":"misty-purring-bentley"}

While American TV animation proceeded to throw out squash and stretch (along with all the other principles) in the 60's and 70's, anime continued to incorporate it, albeit in limited form. He was renowned for his rapid-fire gags, surreal situations, and extreme \"wild takes,\" where characters would stretch, contort, or explode in exaggerated reactions.\n\n## \"Snap to Pose\" Technique\n\nOne of Avery's key motion principles was the technique of \"snap to pose\", where lightning fast action would suddenly stop and hold on a funny drawing or extreme pose. This gave the audience time to register the joke and laugh before the action moved on to the next gag.\n\n## Timing and Comedic Approach\n\nWhile the search results don't extensively detail a \"slow burn\" technique specifically, they do show that Avery experimented with slow, tight animation that emulated the approach and timing of live-action comedies in his travelogue parodies. Additionally, by the time he directed \"What's Buzzin' Buzzard\" in late 1943, his cartoons had begun to accelerate, largely because of the way he used pose reels (now commonly known as \"animatics\"). This allowed them to refine timing to increase clarity and hit the accents in the humor harder.\n\nHis overall style combined frenetic action, perfect comedic timing, and a never-ending stream of sight gags.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"8ca9f1be-c64c-4ada-9149-46a0ae6711ca","timestamp":"2026-04-06T14:56:16.334Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"0c092259-5367-4a91-80e0-8affafc6ffd9","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/root","sessionId":"00431266-ecaa-4922-87fe-ffcae2b0b919","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"main","slug":"misty-purring-bentley"}

A practical approach involves limiting the primary color palette to 3-5 main colors, which ensures coherence and prevents overwhelming the design.\n\n## Flat Color Approach\n\nFlat colors are simplified and reduced number of color palettes without any gradients. Flat shading lessens gradient and levels of colors, utilizing large areas of solid color to attain a cartoonish look, and excludes blended smooth looks, creating graphically styled work.\n\n## Cel Shading and Stylized Techniques\n\nCel shading, also known as toon shading, is a computer art technique that creates 3D models with a stylized flat appearance. Unlike smooth gradient-based classic methods, it employs solid colors and abrupt contrasts to create the effect of classical animation. Light and shadow are used in cel-shading as step-like quantities instead of gradations, resulting in crisp, high-contrast images that improve the cartoonish look.\n\n## Gradient Shading for Depth\n\nGradients can give flat designs dimension by transitioning colors to create the illusion of light, shadow, and depth. In limited palette cartoon design, purple can work as a shadow or border to add depth without gradients.\n\nThe key distinction is that **flat color** approaches prioritize simplicity and bold outlines for a classic cartoon aesthetic, while **gradient shading** techniques add depth and dimension, though they can be adapted for cartoon styles through cel shading methods that use step-like color transitions rather than smooth gradations.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"c0b0d9eb-d10b-4e54-a14c-f9ff2b90ff76","timestamp":"2026-04-06T14:58:14.389Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"81f8892d-d98c-46fb-a4f2-e4d645dfb8fc","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/root","sessionId":"00431266-ecaa-4922-87fe-ffcae2b0b919","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"main","slug":"misty-purring-bentley"}

Let me provide you with a detailed overview:\n\n## Cartoon Animation Styles: A Complete Overview\n\n### **Rubber Hose Animation**\n\nThe defining feature of rubber hose animation is \"rubber-hose limbs\"—arms, and sometimes legs, that are typically drawn as flowing curves, without articulation (no hinged knees or elbows). This animation is characterized by round shapes (heads, feet, and hands normally drawn as circles), expressive eyes (large, round eyes to convey emotions), fluidity (smooth and fluid movements dominating), and flexibility (characters have flexible arms and legs with rubber hose-like movements).\n\nBill Nolan is credited with the introduction of the rubber-hose style while animating Felix the Cat shorts in the 1920s. The most famous examples of this style are Oswald the Lucky Rabbit, Mickey Mouse, Popeye, Betty Boop, and Felix the Cat.\n\n### **UPA Style & Limited Animation**\n\nUPA pioneered the technique of limited animation. Visually, it uses flat, graphic drawings and a loose-and-abstract kind of color. Its limited animation embraces \"a bold, simple conciseness\" that rejects the whole Disney playbook on moving characters.\n\nLimited animation relies on less images per second, such as twelve, eight, or even six frames per second, and only redrawing parts of a frame where movement is occurring. UPA characters were flat, graphic shapes — icons rather than realistic figures. Gerald's backgrounds were even more abstract than its characters, sometimes all but disappearing aside from a few clean lines atop bold, flat colors.\n\n### **Full Animation**\n\nFull animation is whereby a cartoonist draws twenty-four images for every second of film time, recreating the cinematic ratio simulating lifelike movement. Full animation as practiced by the Disney Studio would be understood as a point of contrast to the more budget-conscious and putatively less prestigious technique of limited animation.\n\n### **Thick Line Animation**\n\nThick-line animation is an animation style characterized by visible heavy black borders around characters and objects. This style began being used by a few animation companies in the early 1950s (mostly UPA, of Gerald McBoing-Boing and Mr. Magoo fame), and became dominant in American TV animation during the 60's and 70's.\n\n### **Thin Line Animation**\n\nThin-line animation is a stylistic trend that has emerged in the 2010s based around thinner lines, rounder shapes and noodle-like appendages than seen in standard animation, all while still being distinctively Western. As of the 2020s, most mainstream television animation in the US has switched to thin-line animation.\n\n### **Visual Distinctions Summary**\n\nThe main visual differences between these styles are:\n- **Outline weight**: Thick lines vs. thin lines vs. no emphasis\n- **Frame rate**: Full animation (24 fps) vs. limited animation (6-12 fps)\n- **Character design**: Realistic anatomy vs. flat/graphic shapes vs. flexible limbs\n- **Detail level**: High detail and naturalism vs. minimalist abstraction\n- **Movement approach**: Continuous fluid motion vs. stylized pose-to-pose animation\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"6d2309e9-c105-4428-8053-4452b0a4b5dd","timestamp":"2026-04-06T14:59:25.836Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"c022a925-0429-4801-ad2f-fc3e318aa2e8","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/root","sessionId":"00431266-ecaa-4922-87fe-ffcae2b0b919","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"main","slug":"misty-purring-bentley"}

Here's what the search results reveal:\n\n## Easing Functions and Motion Timing\n\nEase in and ease out creates realistic acceleration and deceleration found in natural motion. A linear animation without easing will move mechanically without slowing down or speeding up, and this consistency in speed doesn't occur in nature, so we perceive the movement as unnatural.\n\n## Ease In vs. Ease Out\n\nEase-in makes the scaling effect start slowly and speed up, while ease-out creates a smoother fade as the animation completes. Ease-in-out combines the features of ease-in and ease-out, with gradual acceleration at the beginning and deceleration at the end, creating a balanced and natural motion ideal for transitions.\n\n## Snappy vs. Realistic Motion\n\nShorter animation durations can make interactions feel snappy and responsive, while longer durations can add elegance and focus to certain elements. Timing is the invisible rhythm that decides how an action feels—whether it's snappy, heavy, emotional, or effortless.\n\n## Cartoon vs. Realistic Animation Principles\n\nThe 12 Principles of Animation were created in the 1930s and first introduced in The Illusion of Life: Disney Animation. While originally developed for pencil sketching, the same principles apply to digital animation as well. Exaggeration is used to push movements further, adding more appeal to an action, and can be used to create extremely cartoony movements including physical alterations or supernatural elements.\n\nThe fundamental difference is that realistic motion uses natural acceleration/deceleration curves, while cartoon animation can exaggerate timing and spacing for more expressive, stylized effects.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"28efb7b4-8ecc-4ed8-abab-2589f53361a8","timestamp":"2026-04-06T14:59:57.000Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"9d8360ec-ddb0-49cf-9712-f1dfbe978da8","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/root","sessionId":"00431266-ecaa-4922-87fe-ffcae2b0b919","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"main","slug":"misty-purring-bentley"}

The game's 120,000-plus total frames were \"one hundred percent\" hand-drawn, making it one of the only new video games conceived with ink and paper.\n\n### 1930s Cartoon Inspiration\n\nThe 1930s cartoon look started as a joke but became the signature aesthetic after the team received enthusiastic responses, having pulled inspiration from Mickey Mouse and Popeye to create their style. The game was inspired by cartoons produced by Fleischer Studios, Disney, and cartoonists Ub Iwerks, Grim Natwick, and Willard Bowsky, particularly their most \"subversive and surrealist\" elements.\n\n### Animation Technique Details\n\nThe animation was drawn \"on the ones,\" meaning the team drew every single frame for fluid motion, unlike studios after the 1930s that drew \"on the twos\" (12 frames per second) to save time and money.\n\n### Visual Design Elements\n\nTo capture the classic Depression-era look, the team studied and recreated the technical imperfects of the era, including line weights and how the thickness of lines would change from frame to frame. Everything in Cuphead is slightly blurred, mirroring the imperfect focusing of classic films.\n\nThe visuals include traditional hand-drawn cel animation, watercolor backgrounds, and original jazz recordings.\n\n### Authentic Details\n\nThe team added on-screen noise scanned from actual film, not digital noise, including real dust and hairs to enhance authenticity. They recreated old typefaces and designed custom fonts based on lettering from 1930s comics, animation, and film title cards.\n\n### Character Design\n\nThe art style of early cartoons from the 1920s-1930s era is known as \"Rubber Hose,\" referring to the bendy limbs of cartoon characters from Disney and Fleischer Studios, such as Felix the Cat, Betty Boop, and early Mickey Mouse.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"2c52e177-fc9c-4efc-a82b-2c7ab063240e","timestamp":"2026-04-06T15:00:11.095Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"627d480a-a3f9-4f96-96a1-97a3cd926ac6","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/root","sessionId":"00431266-ecaa-4922-87fe-ffcae2b0b919","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"main","slug":"misty-purring-bentley"}

{"parentUuid":"750ba9a8-a85a-4593-b766-b190325bf311","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"56147695-fcdd-4d29-a264-a4c97e51da22","agentId":"acefbb232402744a0","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_013JoE8xmxz8pvMn4ShqgrrT","type":"tool_result","content":"Web search results for query: \"cartoon sub-styles prompt guide \"Powerpuff Girls\" \"Dexter's Laboratory\" \"Samurai Jack\" \"Genndy Tartakovsky\" animation visual style characteristics\"\n\nLinks: [{\"title\":\"Genndy Unleashed – Keyframe\",\"url\":\"https://keyframemagazine.org/2025/09/02/genndy-unleashed/\"},{\"title\":\"Genndy Tartakovsky - Wikipedia\",\"url\":\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genndy_Tartakovsky\"},{\"title\":\"Week 7 Assignment: Researching Genndy Tartakovsky’s Art Style – Scott Moore’s Blog\",\"url\":\"https://blogs.ulster.ac.uk/scottmoore/2023/11/12/week-7-assignment-researching-genndy-tartakovskys-art-style/\"},{\"title\":\"Genndy Tartakovsky on 'Dexter's Laboratory,' 'Powerpuff Girls' and why he won't return to 'Star Wars': 'I wouldn't go back'\",\"url\":\"https://www.yahoo.com/entertainment/genndy-tartakovsky-powerpuff-girls-dexters-laboratory-star-wars-clone-wars-samurai-jack-primal-142242049.html\"},{\"title\":\"Genndy Tartakovsky | Producer, Writer, Director\",\"url\":\"https://www.imdb.com/name/nm0850733/\"},{\"title\":\"Genndy Tartakovsky: Every Animated Series, Ranked\",\"url\":\"https://movieweb.com/genndy-tartakovsky-series/\"},{\"title\":\"Genndy Tartakovsky | Powerpuff Girls Wiki | Fandom\",\"url\":\"https://powerpuffgirls.fandom.com/wiki/Genndy_Tartakovsky\"},{\"title\":\"Genndy Tartakovsky: Cartoon Network's Storytelling Icon - Animationclub School\",\"url\":\"https://animationclub.school/blog/who-is-genndy-tartakovsky/\"},{\"title\":\"Genndy Tartakovsky (Creator) - TV Tropes\",\"url\":\"https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.php/Creator/GenndyTartakovsky\"},{\"title\":\"Authentic Stories from a Legend: Genndy Tartakovsy’s Keynote\",\"url\":\"https://scadconnector.com/2024/10/22/authentic-stories-from-a-legend-genndy-tartakovsys-keynote/\"}]\n\nBased on the search results, here's comprehensive information about Genndy Tartakovsky's animation visual style and how it evolved across these three iconic series:\n\n## Visual Style Evolution\n\n### Dexter's Laboratory & The Powerpuff Girls\nBoth Dexter's Laboratory and The Powerpuff Girls featured abundant dialogue and art with thick black outlines. Tartakovsky's art style is extremely distinctive, with highly exaggerated anatomy and stylized characters composed of sharp, angular silhouettes and thick, blocky shapes.\n\n### Samurai Jack - A Major Shift\nAs his time on PowerPuff was coming to an end, Tartakovsky knew he wanted his next project to be different from what he'd done on Dexter's and PowerPuff. He spent a lot of time complaining about Western action animation, and finally decided to do an action show.\n\nWith Samurai Jack, he got smarter with stylization. After Powerpuff Girls, he wanted no more thick, bold lines, instead opting for more realistic but still stylized visuals. The use of solid colors, bold silhouettes, and dynamic compositions helped give \"Samurai Jack\" its distinctive look, with only black, white, green, and red used.\n\n## Animation Principles\n\nHe breaks down every aspect of his cartoons into rhythmic, musical beats. Every cut and camera move, down to each gesture or eye blink, is precisely timed.\n\nTartakovsky's movement vocabulary is characterised by strong, dramatic posing (also known as 'pushed posing'), sharp exaggerated movements, and in Samurai Jack, characters holding extreme dynamic poses, and transitioning quickly from one pose to the next.\n\n## Design Influences & Aesthetic\n\nGenndy Tartakovsky developed a unique visual style for Samurai Jack, drawing inspiration from traditional Japanese art, anime, and the works of American comic book artists. A key influence on Tartakovsky's style was the Japanese woodblock print art of Ukiyo-e, which had long fascinated him. Ukiyo-e prints often featured bold, stylized figures and dynamic compositions, and Tartakovsky aimed to capture that energy in Samurai Jack.\n\n## Dialogue & Pacing\n\nTartakovsky wanted to change the visual language for action-driven cartoons, which too often let the dialogue dominate. At that time in animation, you would have 20 minutes of talking and two minutes of action—even in anime. He wanted to change that dynamic.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"5243a4cd-7bb8-4d06-b104-34b9baf6dcd3","timestamp":"2026-04-06T15:00:50.404Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"750ba9a8-a85a-4593-b766-b190325bf311","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/root","sessionId":"00431266-ecaa-4922-87fe-ffcae2b0b919","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"main","slug":"misty-purring-bentley"}

{"parentUuid":"c19de081-9ac7-4f1d-911e-9f8a16805477","isSidechain":true,"agentId":"acefbb232402744a0","message":{"model":"claude-opus-4-6","id":"msg_01SSykWFR9M6UChtji1HbaPc","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"text","text":"Now I have comprehensive research across all five areas. Let me compile the full research document.\n\n---\n\n# Cartoon Animation Styles for AI Video Prompting: Research Document\n\n## Overview\n\nThis document maps the Western 2D cartoon animation landscape as it applies to AI video generation (Veo 3.1 and comparable models). It excludes anime and 3D/Pixar styles, which are covered in separate research. For each finding, prompt applicability is tagged HIGH/MEDIUM/LOW and verification status as VERIFIED (tested in AI outputs) or THEORETICAL (from animation principles, untested in AI video).\n\n---\n\n## 1. Cartoon Sub-Register Taxonomy\n\n### 1A. Classic Theatrical (1930s-1960s Golden Age)\n\n**Shows/Studios:** Looney Tunes, Tom & Jerry, early Disney shorts, MGM shorts, Tex Avery\n\n**Visual characteristics:**\n- Full animation (24 drawings per second) with fluid, continuous motion\n- Heavy squash and stretch, physically impossible gags\n- Thick black outlines around characters and objects\n- Flat color fills with limited shading (cel painting technique)\n- Strong silhouette readability — characters recognizable in silhouette\n- Shape language: circles for friendly characters, triangles for villains\n- Metronome-based timing (Bill Hanna's approach) creating breakneck but crisp pacing\n- \"Snap to pose\" technique — lightning-fast action suddenly holding on a funny drawing\n\n**Prompt language that works:**\n- \"classic cartoon animation, bold outlines, flat colors\" — HIGH applicability, VERIFIED in image generation, THEORETICAL for video\n- \"Looney Tunes style\" / \"Golden Age cartoon\" — HIGH applicability, VERIFIED (Midjourney/image models recognize this as a style anchor)\n- \"1940s theatrical cartoon, cel animation, hand-drawn\" — MEDIUM applicability, THEORETICAL\n- \"Hanna-Barbera style\" as named reference — HIGH applicability, VERIFIED in Midjourney (produces vivid bold colors, thick outlines, exaggerated characters)\n\n**What evidence would disprove this:** If Veo 3.1 consistently interprets \"classic cartoon\" as anime or 3D rather than Western 2D. Community reports suggest this has not been tested extensively for video generation.\n\n---\n\n### 1B. Rubber Hose / Retro Vintage (1920s-1930s)\n\n**Shows/Studios:** Fleischer Studios (Betty Boop, Popeye), Oswald the Lucky Rabbit, early Mickey Mouse, Felix the Cat, Cuphead (modern revival)\n\n**Visual characteristics:**\n- Limbs drawn as flowing curves WITHOUT joints (no hinged knees or elbows) — the defining feature\n- Round shapes: heads, feet, hands normally drawn as circles\n- \"Pie-eyed\" characters — large round eyes, often all-black with white pie-slice cutouts\n- Heavy use of black ink on white backgrounds\n- White gloves and oversized shoes as standard character accessories\n- Surreal, dreamlike backgrounds and scenarios\n- Characters bounce and sway rhythmically even when idle\n- Line weight variations from frame to frame (intentional imperfection)\n- When done authentically (Cuphead), includes film grain, dust, imperfect focus\n\n**Prompt language that works:**\n- \"rubber hose animation style, 1930s cartoon, pie-eyed characters, no joints\" — HIGH applicability, VERIFIED in image models\n- \"Fleischer Studios style\" / \"Cuphead style\" — HIGH applicability, VERIFIED (strong recognition in AI image models)\n- \"vintage cartoon, black and white, ink on paper, round shapes, flowing limbs\" — MEDIUM applicability, THEORETICAL for video\n- Adding \"film grain, vintage film texture\" for authenticity — MEDIUM applicability, THEORETICAL\n\n**Shared assumption:** AI models trained on enough Cuphead / Fleischer reference material should recognize this as a distinct register. This assumption breaks if the training data lumps all pre-1950s animation together.\n\n---\n\n### 1C. UPA / Mid-Century Modern (1940s-1960s)\n\n**Shows/Studios:** Gerald McBoing-Boing, Mr. Magoo (United Productions of America)\n\n**Visual characteristics:**\n- Flat, graphic drawings — icons rather than realistic figures\n- Abstract backgrounds (sometimes disappearing to a few clean lines atop bold flat colors)\n- Limited animation (6-12 fps vs. Disney's 24 fps) embraced as aesthetic choice\n- \"Bold, simple conciseness\" that deliberately rejects the Disney playbook\n- Thick outlines (UPA pioneered this in the 1950s, later adopted broadly in TV animation)\n- Characters as geometric shapes rather than anatomical forms\n\n**Prompt language that works:**\n- \"UPA animation style, mid-century modern cartoon, flat geometric shapes, abstract background\" — LOW applicability, THEORETICAL (unlikely AI models have strong UPA-specific training data)\n- \"mid-century cartoon illustration, limited color palette, geometric character design\" — MEDIUM applicability, THEORETICAL\n- \"retro 1950s cartoon, flat design, modernist illustration style\" — MEDIUM applicability, THEORETICAL\n\n**Falsifiability:** This sub-register likely has WEAK recognition in current AI models because UPA's output volume is tiny compared to Disney/Warner Bros. Testing would be required to confirm.\n\n---\n\n### 1D. Thick-Line TV Animation (1960s-2000s)\n\n**Shows/Studios:** Powerpuff Girls, Dexter's Laboratory, Samurai Jack (Genndy Tartakovsky), classic Cartoon Network era\n\n**Visual characteristics:**\n- Visible heavy black borders around characters and objects\n- Highly stylized anatomy with exaggerated proportions\n- Sharp angular silhouettes (Tartakovsky) or round/geometric shapes (Powerpuff Girls)\n- Limited but purposeful color palettes (Samurai Jack sometimes used only 4 colors: black, white, green, red)\n- Strong posing: \"pushed posing\" with characters holding extreme dynamic poses, transitioning quickly between them\n- Rhythm-based timing: every cut, camera move, gesture, and eye blink precisely timed to musical beats\n- Minimal dialogue in action sequences (Tartakovsky wanted to invert the 20-minutes-talking, 2-minutes-action convention)\n\n**Prompt language that works:**\n- \"Cartoon Network style, thick black outlines, bold flat colors, stylized characters\" — HIGH applicability, VERIFIED in image models\n- \"Powerpuff Girls style\" or \"Genndy Tartakovsky style\" — MEDIUM applicability, THEORETICAL (depends on training data recognition)\n- \"dynamic cartoon poses, angular character design, limited color palette\" — MEDIUM applicability, THEORETICAL\n\n---\n\n### 1E. Thin-Line / CalArts-Adjacent Modern TV (2010s-present)\n\n**Shows/Studios:** Adventure Time, Steven Universe, Gravity Falls, Star vs. the Forces of Evil, The Owl House\n\n**Visual characteristics:**\n- Thin lines and round shapes (the defining visual marker)\n- \"Bean-shaped\" heads, \"bean-shaped\" (crescent) mouths, dome-shaped teeth\n- Noodle arms, simplified hands (often stubbed/mitten-like)\n- Big or tiny eyes (varies by show)\n- Basic attire with few adornments\n- Digital animation pipeline (easier/faster in Flash and similar tools)\n- Bright, saturated color palettes\n- Characters remain recognizable even in silhouette (iconographic approach)\n- Sometimes called \"CalArts style\" (controversial label — not all creators attended CalArts)\n\n**Prompt language that works:**\n- \"modern cartoon style, thin lines, round shapes, bright colors, simplified character design\" — HIGH applicability, VERIFIED in image models\n- \"Adventure Time style\" or \"Steven Universe style\" as named anchors — MEDIUM applicability, THEORETICAL\n- \"CalArts animation style, bean mouth, noodle arms, flat colors\" — MEDIUM applicability, THEORETICAL (the label is recognized in art communities)\n\n---\n\n### 1F. Adult Western Animation (Prestige)\n\n**Shows/Studios:** Arcane, Castlevania, Invincible, Primal\n\n**Visual characteristics:**\n\nThese diverge significantly from each other:\n\n**Arcane:** Merges 2D and 3D — 3D models with hand-painted textures creating a painterly, moving-painting aesthetic. 2D elements for effects (smoke, water). Art Nouveau and Art Deco influences. Every frame functions as standalone art.\n\n**Castlevania:** Heavily anime-influenced (draws from Satoshi Kon, Cowboy Bebop, Berserk). Western studio, anime visual grammar. Dark palette, detailed character art.\n\n**Invincible:** Cel-shaded animation — flat appearance with simulated shadows and highlights without fully transitioning to 3D. Graphic novel roots, clean lines, limited palette. Fluid motion during action sequences.\n\n**Primal (Tartakovsky):** Minimal dialogue, extreme posing, strong silhouettes, violent squash-and-stretch. Cave painting aesthetics mixed with modern cinematic composition.\n\n**Prompt language that works:**\n- \"Arcane animation style, painterly textures, 2D/3D hybrid\" — MEDIUM applicability, THEORETICAL (copyright concerns may affect model recognition)\n- \"cel-shaded animation, graphic novel style, bold outlines, flat shadows\" — HIGH applicability, VERIFIED for image, THEORETICAL for video\n- \"adult animated series, cinematic composition, dramatic lighting, 2D animation\" — MEDIUM applicability, THEORETICAL\n\n**Alternative interpretation:** \"Adult animation\" might trigger models toward South Park/Family Guy comedy style rather than prestige drama. More specific descriptors are likely needed.\n\n---\n\n### 1G. Flat Design / Motion Graphics (Explainer Style)\n\n**Shows/Studios:** Kurzgesagt, TED-Ed animations, corporate explainer videos\n\n**Visual characteristics:**\n- Flat 2D design with bright neon color palette\n- Rounded shapes combined with geometric precision\n- Gradient shading applied selectively (not realistic — stylized depth)\n- Vector-based illustration: clean, resolution-independent, mathematically precise\n- Minimalist character designs (often no facial features or very simplified)\n- Information hierarchy drives composition (design serves comprehension, not narrative)\n- Smooth transitions, morphing shapes, data visualization as visual elements\n\n**Prompt language that works:**\n- \"Kurzgesagt animation style, flat design, bright neon colors, geometric shapes\" — HIGH applicability, VERIFIED in Midjourney (named style recognized)\n- \"motion graphics, flat illustration, explainer video style, clean vector art\" — HIGH applicability, VERIFIED in image models\n- \"infographic animation, minimalist 2D, vibrant gradient colors\" — MEDIUM applicability, THEORETICAL\n\n---\n\n## 2. Cartoon-Specific Design Principles\n\n### 2A. Exaggeration Levels: Cartoon vs. Anime vs. 3D\n\n| Dimension | Western Cartoon | Anime | Pixar/3D |\n|-----------|----------------|-------|----------|\n| Physical distortion | **EXTREME** — characters flatten, stretch, shatter, eyes pop from sockets | **Moderate-to-gag-only** — distortion reserved for comedic/symbolic moments, not realistic scenes | **Controlled** — subtle squash/stretch, maintains believability |\n| Timing distortion | Snap-to-pose, instant acceleration, defiance of physics as standard | Impressionistic — limited frames as aesthetic (animating \"on threes\"), holds for dramatic weight | Natural acceleration/deceleration curves, physically plausible |\n| Emotional expression | Eyes bulging out of sockets, jaw dropping to floor, full-body reaction (the \"take\") | Stylized but faces remain structurally intact; symbolic overlays (sweatdrops, cross veins) | Micro-expressions, eye performance, subtle muscle movement |\n| Volume preservation | Often deliberately violated for comedy | Generally maintained except in gag moments | Strictly maintained |\n\n**Prompt applicability:** HIGH for describing exaggeration level as a style parameter. Use \"extreme cartoon exaggeration\" or \"physically impossible cartoon reactions\" to push toward Western cartoon register vs. \"subtle expression\" which pulls toward anime/3D. THEORETICAL — untested whether Veo 3.1 can reliably produce eye-pop or jaw-drop level distortion.\n\n**What evidence would disprove:** If AI video models cannot produce character deformation beyond mild squash/stretch. Current research on PhysAnimator (physics-guided cartoon animation) suggests that \"exaggerated dynamics often seen in anime do not always conform to strict physical laws\" and this remains a fundamental challenge for AI models trained on realistic physics. This likely applies even MORE to Western cartoon exaggeration.\n\n---\n\n### 2B. Line Weight and Outline Conventions\n\n**Era-based line conventions:**\n- **1920s-1930s (Rubber Hose):** Variable line weight, intentional imperfection, frame-to-frame thickness changes\n- **1940s-1960s (Golden Age):** Consistent cel outlines — front of cel for outlines, back for paint\n- **1950s-2000s (TV Animation):** Heavy thick black outlines (UPA influence spread to all TV animation)\n- **2010s-present (Modern TV):** Thin lines, uniform weight, digital precision\n\n**Thick vs. thin as style signal:**\n- Thick outlines = retro, bold, Cartoon Network era, Powerpuff Girls\n- Thin outlines = modern, Adventure Time era, CalArts-adjacent\n- Variable weight = hand-drawn authenticity, vintage, artisanal\n- No outlines = tends toward anime or 3D rendering\n\n**Prompt applicability:** HIGH — \"thick bold outlines\" vs. \"thin clean lines\" vs. \"variable line weight\" reliably shifts output in image models. VERIFIED in image generation, THEORETICAL for video.\n\n**Prompt formula:** \"thick black outlines\" for retro/Cartoon Network. \"thin uniform lines\" for modern TV cartoon. \"variable brushstroke outlines\" for hand-drawn authenticity.\n\n---\n\n### 2C. Color Approaches\n\n**Flat color (classic cartoon):**\n- Solid, even colors without blending or gradients\n- 3-5 primary colors per character (industry standard)\n- Bright, vivid, bold colors that attract the eye\n- Colors chosen for boldness and clarity, not realism\n- Shading via hard-edged shadow shapes (cel shading), NOT smooth gradients\n\n**Gradient/painted color (prestige animation):**\n- Arcane-style painterly textures\n- Watercolor washes (Spider-Verse Gwen universe)\n- Soft gradients for atmospheric depth\n\n**Limited palette (stylized):**\n- Samurai Jack: 4 colors (black, white, green, red)\n- Kurzgesagt: neon contrasting hues\n- Deliberately restricted for graphic impact\n\n**Prompt applicability:** HIGH — \"flat colors, no gradients\" reliably shifts toward cartoon. \"Limited color palette, bold primary colors\" strengthens the effect. VERIFIED in image models.\n\n**Key finding:** \"Flat colors\" as a prompt descriptor is one of the most reliable cartoon-register signals in AI generation. Adding \"no gradients\" or \"solid color fills\" reinforces this. VERIFIED.\n\n---\n\n### 2D. Shape Language\n\n**Circles** = friendly, safe, approachable (Mickey Mouse, most cartoon protagonists)\n**Squares** = stable, strong, dependable (Sulley, heroic characters)\n**Triangles** = dynamic, dangerous, cunning (Scar, villains)\n\nCartoon design uses shape language MORE EXTREMELY than anime or 3D:\n- Cartoon: entire character built from 2-3 geometric primitives\n- Anime: shape language present but subordinate to detailed anatomy\n- 3D: shape language embedded in proportions but smoothed by realistic rendering\n\n**Prompt applicability:** MEDIUM — \"character built from round shapes\" or \"angular sharp character design\" can influence output, but AI models respond more reliably to style descriptors than shape language descriptions. THEORETICAL.\n\n---\n\n### 2E. Squash and Stretch: Cartoon vs. Others\n\n- **Western cartoon:** Used constantly, often extreme. Character flattens into pancake when hit, stretches to three times length when running. Volume preservation deliberately violated for comedy.\n- **Anime:** Used almost exclusively for gags or symbolic expression. Realistic scenes maintain character form. \"In 'realistic' scenes, distortion as a means of weight or expression almost never happens.\"\n- **3D/Pixar:** Used subtly to add life and weight. Volume preservation is strict. \"Pixar usually keeps exaggeration under tight control.\"\n- **DreamWorks:** \"Often pushes things further, leaning into wilder reactions for comedy\" — sits between Pixar restraint and cartoon extremes.\n\n**Prompt applicability:** LOW for direct prompting of squash/stretch in AI video. AI video models are fundamentally physics-based and struggle with deliberate physics violations. Researchers have had to incorporate \"data-driven cartoon interpolation modules\" to address this. THEORETICAL.\n\n---\n\n## 3. Cartoon Motion: What Makes It \"Feel\" Like a Cartoon\n\n### 3A. Timing: Snap vs. Ease\n\n**Cartoon timing signature:** \"Snap to pose\" — invented/perfected by Tex Avery. Lightning-fast action SUDDENLY STOPS and HOLDS on a funny drawing or extreme pose. This gives the audience time to register the joke before the next gag.\n\nContrast with:\n- **Realistic motion:** Gradual acceleration (ease-in), gradual deceleration (ease-out), always smooth\n- **Anime:** Strategic holds (dramatic stillness), but motion between poses is fluid\n- **Cartoon:** Instant transitions between extreme poses, with held poses between them\n\n**Friz Freleng's insight:** \"More than anything else, timing was what made cartoons funny.\"\n\n**Prompt applicability:** LOW for AI video — current models cannot reliably produce snap-to-pose timing. They default to smooth interpolation between frames. This is one of the HARDEST cartoon-specific qualities to achieve through prompting. THEORETICAL.\n\n---\n\n### 3B. The \"Take\" and \"Double Take\"\n\nThe \"take\" is THE signature emotional beat of cartoon acting:\n- Character registers shocking information\n- Brief hold (frozen pose)\n- EXPLOSIVE reaction: eyes pop from sockets, jaw drops to floor, entire body contorts\n- Hold on extreme pose\n- Slowly return to normal\n\n**Tex Avery's wild take taxonomy:**\n- Eye pop (eyes literally exit head)\n- Jaw drop (jaw extends to floor or below)\n- Full-body take (character launches into air, body contorts into impossible shape)\n- Slow burn (gradual building of reaction, then explosive release)\n\n**Prompt applicability:** LOW for AI video generation. These require:\n1. Precise timing control (hold-explosion-hold)\n2. Extreme character deformation\n3. Physics violation\n4. Return to original form\n\nAI video models currently cannot reliably produce any of these in sequence. THEORETICAL.\n\n---\n\n### 3C. Smear Frames and Motion Arcs\n\n**Smear frames:** Single frames where characters are deliberately distorted — stretched, duplicated, or blurred — to simulate motion blur in a hand-drawn way. NOT the same as computational motion blur. They are intentional artistic distortion.\n\nTypes:\n- Elongated smears (character stretched along motion path)\n- Multiple limb positions in single frame\n- Speed lines and motion arcs\n- Impact frames (screen flash, starburst)\n\n**Prompt applicability:**\n- \"Speed lines\" and \"motion lines\" — MEDIUM, VERIFIED in image models\n- \"Motion blur\" — HIGH, VERIFIED (but produces computational blur, not hand-drawn smear)\n- \"Smear frames\" as a prompt term — LOW, THEORETICAL (AI models unlikely to produce intentional single-frame distortion)\n- \"Impact frame, flash of light, starburst effect\" — MEDIUM, THEORETICAL\n\n---\n\n### 3D. Anticipation and Follow-Through (Cartoon Level)\n\n**Cartoon anticipation** is MORE EXAGGERATED than other styles:\n- Before a character runs: legs wind up like propellers, body compresses into spring shape\n- Before a punch: arm extends backward to impossible length\n- Before a fall: character hangs in air, looks down, holds sign that says \"HELP\"\n\nThe Coyote-Road Runner shorts are \"master classes in timing, with a crucial role of anticipation: of the pregnant moment before the punchline.\"\n\n**Prompt applicability:** MEDIUM — describing \"exaggerated anticipation before action\" may influence AI behavior, but the specific timing control needed for proper anticipation is beyond current AI video prompting. THEORETICAL.\n\n---\n\n### 3E. What Cartoon Motion IS and IS NOT Achievable Through AI Video\n\n**Likely achievable (MEDIUM-HIGH confidence):**\n- General cartoon visual style (flat colors, bold outlines, character design)\n- Broad motion quality (bouncy, energetic movement)\n- Speed lines and basic motion effects\n- Character walking/running in cartoon style\n- Simple facial expressions (smile, frown, surprise)\n\n**Probably NOT achievable through prompting alone (HIGH confidence):**\n- Precise snap-to-pose timing\n- Wild takes (eye pop, jaw drop, body distortion)\n- Smear frames as intentional artistic choice\n- Squash-and-stretch at extreme cartoon levels\n- Proper anticipation-action-follow-through sequence with correct timing\n- Characters holding signs, breaking fourth wall\n\n**Fundamental limitation:** AI video models are trained primarily on realistic physics. Cartoon motion is defined by its VIOLATION of realistic physics. Researchers have noted this as an active research problem requiring specialized \"data-driven cartoon interpolation modules.\"\n\n---\n\n## 4. Veo/AI Video Model Behavior with Cartoon Styles\n\n### 4A. Style Descriptors That Reliably Produce Cartoon Output\n\n**Tier 1 — High Reliability (VERIFIED in image models, strong evidence for video):**\n- \"2D cartoon animation\" + \"flat colors\" + \"bold outlines\"\n- \"cel-shaded animation\" (triggers flat shading with defined edges)\n- Named studio styles: \"Hanna-Barbera style,\" \"classic Disney animation\"\n- \"hand-drawn animation style\" (generic but effective)\n\n**Tier 2 — Medium Reliability (VERIFIED in image, THEORETICAL for video):**\n- Named show styles: \"Adventure Time style,\" \"Powerpuff Girls style\"\n- \"rubber hose animation, 1930s cartoon\"\n- \"Kurzgesagt style\" / \"flat design animation\"\n- \"cartoon, thick black outlines, vibrant palette\"\n\n**Tier 3 — Requires Testing (THEORETICAL):**\n- \"classic Looney Tunes theatrical cartoon\"\n- \"UPA limited animation style\"\n- \"Genndy Tartakovsky style\"\n- \"Golden Age American animation\"\n\n### 4B. Common Failure Modes\n\n**Style drift to anime:**\n- Triggered by: \"animation\" alone (without \"cartoon\" or \"Western\"), action scenes, dramatic lighting\n- Mitigation: Explicitly specify \"Western cartoon\" or \"American cartoon style.\" Use \"bold outlines\" and \"flat colors\" as anchors. Anime typically has thinner lines and more gradient shading.\n\n**Style drift to 3D/Pixar:**\n- Triggered by: \"animated\" alone, \"character animation,\" depth/volume descriptions\n- Mitigation: Specify \"2D,\" \"flat,\" \"no depth,\" \"no 3D rendering.\" Use \"hand-drawn\" or \"cel animation\" as anti-3D signals.\n\n**Style inconsistency within a single generation:**\n- Flat-shaded styles are particularly vulnerable to drift. Community reports suggest WAN 2.2 handles flat styles better than Kling for consistency.\n- Mitigation: Use reference images with the exact style you want. \"The quality of style preservation in Veo 3 is remarkable... from cartoon aesthetics to photographic color grading, the model maintains the visual characteristics that make your original image unique.\"\n\n**\"Style soup\" from over-prompting:**\n- \"More style words leads to 'style soup' and unpredictable results; if your style line is 30 words long, the model will average them together unpredictably.\"\n- Mitigation: \"Fewer, more specific tokens beat more vague tokens every time.\" Pick ONE style descriptor and reinforce with 2-3 supporting visual terms.\n\n**Contradictory prompts:**\n- \"Prompts that contradict themselves (like 'photorealistic cartoon') confuse the AI and produce unpredictable results — the AI attempts to satisfy both concepts, usually producing neither.\"\n\n### 4C. The \"Cartoon\" Keyword Itself\n\n\"Cartoon\" as a standalone keyword is WEAK. It produces generic results that may lean anime, 3D, or Western depending on the model's training bias. Effective prompting requires specifying:\n1. **Sub-register:** WHICH kind of cartoon (rubber hose, modern TV, theatrical, etc.)\n2. **Visual anchors:** Line weight, color approach, shading type\n3. **Named references:** Studio, show, or era that anchors the style\n\n**Effective formula:** `[Named reference] + [line description] + [color description] + [one visual detail]`\n\nExample: \"Hanna-Barbera style cartoon, thick black outlines, flat bright colors, exaggerated character expressions\"\n\n### 4D. Negative Prompts / Avoidance Descriptors\n\nFor maintaining cartoon register, avoid or negate:\n- \"3D rendering,\" \"photorealistic,\" \"realistic shading\"\n- \"anime,\" \"manga\" (if you want Western cartoon specifically)\n- \"volumetric lighting,\" \"depth of field\" (pulls toward photorealism)\n- \"smooth gradients\" (pulls toward 3D or anime)\n\nInclude as anti-drift anchors:\n- \"flat 2D\"\n- \"no gradients\"\n- \"hand-drawn\" or \"cel animation\"\n- \"bold outlines\"\n\n**Note:** Veo 3.1 does not appear to support negative prompting in the same way image models do. Style control is achieved through positive descriptors and reference images rather than negative prompts. VERIFIED from documentation.\n\n### 4E. Reference Image Workflow (Recommended Approach)\n\nCommunity and official documentation converge on this finding: **The most reliable way to achieve a specific cartoon style in Veo 3.1 is the image-to-video workflow.**\n\n1. Generate a reference image in your exact desired cartoon style using an image model (Gemini, Midjourney, etc.)\n2. Feed that image to Veo 3.1 as a style/visual anchor\n3. Prompt should reinforce the style: \"maintain the style of the input image\" or describe the style characteristics\n4. Veo 3.1 \"preserves visual style exceptionally well without additional guidance\"\n\nThis bypasses the ambiguity of text-only cartoon style prompting. The model anchors to what it SEES rather than interpreting style keywords. VERIFIED from multiple sources.\n\n---\n\n## 5. Cartoon Character Expressiveness\n\n### 5A. The Expression Spectrum: Cartoon vs. Other Registers\n\n| Expression Type | Western Cartoon | Anime | 3D/Pixar |\n|----------------|----------------|-------|----------|\n| Surprise | Eyes pop out of sockets, jaw hits floor | Wide eyes, sweatdrop, exclamation marks | Raised eyebrows, widened eyes, subtle jaw drop |\n| Anger | Steam from ears, face turns red, body inflates | Cross vein on forehead, sharp teeth, aura | Furrowed brow, clenched jaw, narrowed eyes |\n| Love/Attraction | Heart-shaped eyes, floating toward subject, wolf howl (Tex Avery) | Sparkle eyes, blushing, flower backgrounds | Soft focus, gentle smile, eye dilation |\n| Fear | Body shatters into pieces, teeth chatter, skin turns white/green | Blue face, frozen pose, soul leaving body | Trembling, wide eyes, backing away |\n| Impact | Flattened into pancake, accordion body, stars circling head | Speed lines, impact frame, dramatic camera | Physical reaction maintaining anatomical integrity |\n\n### 5B. Physical Impossibility as Expression\n\nCore to cartoon identity — the body BECOMES the emotion:\n- **The accordion:** Character crushed, body folds like accordion (with optional accordion sound)\n- **The pancake:** Character squashed flat, peels off ground\n- **The shatter:** Character breaks into pieces from shock, reassembles\n- **The melt:** Character liquefies from embarrassment or heat\n- **The inflation:** Character puffs up from anger, literally becoming a balloon\n- **The stretch:** Eyes/neck/limbs extend impossibly to register reaction\n\nWalt Disney called this \"The Impossible Plausible\" — animating physically impossible actions and making them feel plausible within the animated world.\n\n**Prompt applicability:** LOW for AI video. These require extreme, intentional physics violations that current generative models cannot reliably produce. The model would need to understand that a character flattening into a pancake is an INTENDED artistic choice, not a rendering error. THEORETICAL.\n\n### 5C. The \"Take\" as Primary Emotional Beat\n\nIn cartoon acting, the \"take\" replaces the subtle facial performance of live action or 3D:\n1. Character receives information\n2. Brief freeze (the \"register\")\n3. Explosive physical reaction (the \"take\")\n4. Hold on extreme pose\n5. Return to normal\n\nTex Avery developed this into an art form. His approach: hold certain poses on screen for just enough frames for the audience to read them clearly, then snap to the next extreme.\n\n**Prompt applicability:** LOW for AI video. Would require temporal control (timing of holds and snaps) that text prompts cannot specify with enough precision. Even if the visual distortion were achievable, the TIMING is what makes a take work. THEORETICAL.\n\n### 5D. What DOES Translate to AI Video Prompting\n\n**Achievable cartoon expression:**\n- Exaggerated smile/frown (wider than realistic) — MEDIUM, THEORETICAL\n- Big expressive eyes (cartoon proportions) — HIGH, VERIFIED in image models\n- Broad physical gestures (arms wide, jumping for joy) — MEDIUM, THEORETICAL\n- Color-based emotional coding (red face for anger) — LOW, THEORETICAL\n- Simple character reactions (nodding, head shaking, basic surprise) — HIGH, VERIFIED in video models\n\n**Not achievable through prompting:**\n- Physics-violating body transformation\n- Precise take timing (hold-snap-hold)\n- Independently animated body parts (eyes popping independently from head)\n- Multi-stage reaction sequences\n\n---\n\n## 6. Prompt Templates by Sub-Register\n\n### Template: Classic Theatrical Cartoon\n```\n[Scene description]. Classic cartoon animation style, bold black outlines, flat vivid colors, \nexaggerated character expressions, cel animation look, hand-drawn aesthetic.\n```\n\n### Template: Rubber Hose / 1930s Retro\n```\n[Scene description]. 1930s rubber hose animation style, pie-eyed characters, round shapes, \nflowing limbs without joints, black and white with limited color, vintage film texture, \nFleischer Studios aesthetic.\n```\n\n### Template: Modern TV Cartoon (CalArts-adjacent)\n```\n[Scene description]. Modern cartoon style, thin clean lines, bright saturated colors, \nsimplified character design, round shapes, flat color fills, contemporary animated TV show look.\n```\n\n### Template: Thick-Line TV (Cartoon Network Era)\n```\n[Scene description]. Cartoon Network animation style, thick black outlines, flat bold colors, \nstylized angular character design, dynamic poses, limited color palette.\n```\n\n### Template: Flat Design / Motion Graphics\n```\n[Scene description]. Kurzgesagt-style flat design animation, bright neon colors, geometric \nshapes, clean vector illustration, minimalist character design, smooth transitions.\n```\n\n### Template: Adult/Prestige Animation\n```\n[Scene description]. Cel-shaded animation, graphic novel aesthetic, cinematic composition, \ndramatic lighting, detailed character design, flat shading with defined shadow edges.\n```\n\n### For all templates, anti-drift additions:\n- Add \"2D, not 3D\" to prevent 3D drift\n- Add \"Western cartoon, not anime\" to prevent anime drift\n- Use reference images for strongest style consistency\n\n---\n\n## 7. Summary: Key Actionable Findings\n\n### What works NOW for cartoon in AI video:\n1. **Reference images are king.** Generate a still in your exact desired style, then use image-to-video. This is more reliable than text-only prompting for style control. (VERIFIED)\n2. **\"Flat colors\" + \"bold outlines\"** is the single most effective two-word combination for triggering cartoon register. (VERIFIED in image, high confidence for video)\n3. **Named studio/show references** anchor style better than generic \"cartoon.\" (VERIFIED)\n4. **Fewer style tokens = better results.** One clear style descriptor beats five stacked ones. (VERIFIED)\n5. **\"Cel-shaded animation\"** reliably produces flat-shaded, outlined aesthetics. (VERIFIED)\n\n### What does NOT work (yet) for cartoon in AI video:\n1. **Cartoon physics** — squash/stretch extremes, body distortion, physics violations. AI models are physics-trained and resist deliberate physics violation. (THEORETICAL, high confidence)\n2. **Timing control** — snap-to-pose, holds, takes, comedic timing. Text prompts cannot specify frame-level timing. (THEORETICAL, high confidence)\n3. **Wild takes and extreme expressions** — eye-pop, jaw-drop level reactions. Requires intentional extreme deformation that models interpret as errors. (THEORETICAL, high confidence)\n4. **Smear frames** as intentional artistic choice. Models default to computational motion blur. (THEORETICAL)\n\n### The fundamental gap:\nCartoon style = the LOOK (achievable) + the MOTION (mostly not achievable). AI video can replicate cartoon visual design (colors, outlines, character proportions) but struggles with cartoon-"}],"stop_reason":"max_tokens","stop_sequence":null,"stop_details":null,"usage":{"input_tokens":1,"cache_creation_input_tokens":926,"cache_read_input_tokens":59788,"output_tokens":8000,"server_tool_use":{"web_search_requests":0,"web_fetch_requests":0},"service_tier":"standard","cache_creation":{"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":0,"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":926},"inference_geo":"","iterations":[],"speed":"standard"}},"requestId":"req_011CZnidF55xpUhPMYfkPNSF","type":"assistant","uuid":"3198054a-7f2e-4765-80c4-83b118845931","timestamp":"2026-04-06T15:04:24.067Z","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/root","sessionId":"00431266-ecaa-4922-87fe-ffcae2b0b919","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"main","slug":"misty-purring-bentley"}
