{"parentUuid":"dd64970c-7a49-4b23-a169-4e11dbdc6db3","isSidechain":true,"agentId":"acefbb232402744a0","message":{"model":"claude-opus-4-6","id":"msg_011uniCdhAu3gtseLkuz3ohr","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"text","text":"Now I have comprehensive research across all five areas. Let me compile the full research document.\n\n---\n\n# Cartoon Animation Styles for AI Video Prompting: Research Document\n\n## Scope & Method\n\nThis document maps the Western 2D cartoon register -- styles that are neither anime nor 3D/Pixar -- and evaluates their applicability to AI text-to-video generation (Veo 3.1 and comparable models). Research was conducted via web search across animation history resources, AI art communities, prompt engineering guides, and official model documentation.\n\n---\n\n## 1. CARTOON SUB-REGISTER TAXONOMY\n\n### 1A. Classic Theatrical / Golden Age (1930s-1960s)\n\n**Key exemplars:** Looney Tunes, Tom & Jerry, early Disney shorts, MGM cartoons\n\n**Visual characteristics:**\n- Full animation (24 unique drawings per second)\n- Heavy black outlines with variable line weight\n- Saturated, flat colors with minimal gradients\n- Extreme squash-and-stretch deformation\n- Characters with strong silhouettes, exaggerated anatomy\n- Detailed painted backgrounds contrasting with simpler character art\n\n**Motion characteristics:**\n- Snap-to-pose timing (Tex Avery's signature: lightning-fast action freezing on a held extreme pose)\n- Wild takes (eyes popping from sockets, jaw dropping to floor, body shattering)\n- Metronome-based timing (Bill Hanna's approach for Tom & Jerry: precise, rhythmic beats)\n- Anticipation is large and exaggerated -- the bigger the anticipation, the more comedic the result\n\n**Prompt applicability:** MEDIUM. The term \"classic cartoon\" or \"Golden Age animation style\" is recognized by AI models but tends to produce a generalized retro look rather than the specific Warner Bros. or MGM register. Named studios (\"Looney Tunes style\", \"Tom and Jerry style\") may be filtered by some models due to IP concerns. \"Theatrical cartoon animation, 1940s, full animation, bold outlines, saturated flat colors\" is a more reliable descriptor chain.\n\n**Verification status:** THEORETICAL for Veo specifically; VERIFIED in image generators (Midjourney produces recognizable Golden Age aesthetics from these descriptors).\n\n---\n\n### 1B. Rubber Hose / Vintage (1920s-1930s)\n\n**Key exemplars:** Fleischer Studios (Betty Boop, early Popeye), early Mickey Mouse, Felix the Cat, Cuphead (modern revival)\n\n**Visual characteristics:**\n- Limbless joints -- arms and legs are smooth, flowing curves with no elbows or knees\n- \"Pie-eyed\" characters (round, black-and-white eyes)\n- White gloves and oversized shoes as standard character accessories\n- Heavy black ink on white or minimal backgrounds\n- Round shapes dominating all character designs (circles for heads, hands, feet)\n- Slight blur and grain (mimicking 1930s film stock)\n- Surreal, dreamlike scenarios (characters interact with their own world's physics)\n\n**Motion characteristics:**\n- Everything bounces and sways rhythmically, often synced to music\n- Characters and objects are interchangeably alive\n- Fluidity is prioritized over anatomical accuracy\n- The world itself is elastic -- background objects squash and stretch\n\n**Prompt applicability:** HIGH. \"Rubber hose animation style\" is a well-recognized descriptor in AI image and video models. \"1930s cartoon style, rubber hose limbs, pie-eyed characters, black and white\" produces consistent results. \"Cuphead style\" also works well as a contemporary reference anchor. Adding \"vintage film grain, slight blur\" reinforces period accuracy.\n\n**Verification status:** VERIFIED in image generators. THEORETICAL for Veo video generation (the style is visually distinctive enough that text-to-video models can lock onto it, but motion fidelity to rubber hose conventions is unconfirmed).\n\n---\n\n### 1C. UPA / Mid-Century Modern (1940s-1960s)\n\n**Key exemplars:** Gerald McBoing-Boing, Mr. Magoo, early Hanna-Barbera (The Flintstones, The Jetsons)\n\n**Visual characteristics:**\n- Flat, graphic, abstract designs -- characters are icons, not naturalistic figures\n- Limited color palettes with bold, deliberate color choices\n- Backgrounds that can be almost entirely abstract (a few clean lines atop flat colors)\n- Thick outlines (UPA pioneered the thick-line convention in TV animation)\n- Geometric simplification of all forms\n- Strong influence from modern art, graphic design, and illustration\n\n**Motion characteristics:**\n- Limited animation (6-12 fps) -- deliberately economical movement\n- Characters hold poses longer; movement is selective and purposeful\n- \"Bold, simple conciseness\" -- rejects Disney's full-animation playbook\n- Personality comes from design and timing, not fluid motion\n\n**Prompt applicability:** MEDIUM-LOW. \"UPA animation style\" is not widely recognized by AI models. More effective descriptors: \"mid-century modern cartoon, flat graphic design, limited animation, bold geometric shapes, abstract backgrounds, 1950s cartoon.\" For Hanna-Barbera specifically: \"Hanna-Barbera style, 1960s cartoon, bold lines, flat color, minimal movement, strong silhouettes.\"\n\n**Verification status:** VERIFIED for Hanna-Barbera in image generators (Midlibrary documents this as a recognized Midjourney style). THEORETICAL for video generation.\n\n---\n\n### 1D. Modern TV Animation / CalArts-Adjacent (2010s-present)\n\n**Key exemplars:** Adventure Time, Steven Universe, Gravity Falls, Star vs. the Forces of Evil, The Owl House\n\n**Visual characteristics:**\n- Thin-line animation (thin outlines, rounder shapes, noodle-like limbs)\n- \"Bean-shaped\" heads with \"bean-shaped\" mouths and dome-shaped teeth\n- Simplified features: dot-and-line mouths, large or tiny eyes\n- Bright, saturated color palettes\n- Digital production (Flash/Toon Boom aesthetic)\n- Characters are readable as silhouettes at any size (iconographic design)\n\n**Motion characteristics:**\n- More limited than theatrical but smoother than classic TV animation\n- Digital tweening creates fluid basic motion\n- Expressive acting through simple, clean poses\n- Less physical impossibility than Golden Age; more grounded emotional expression\n\n**Prompt applicability:** HIGH. \"Modern cartoon style, thin lines, round shapes, bright colors, digital animation\" produces recognizable results. Naming specific shows works when models allow it. \"Cartoon Network style\" is a useful umbrella descriptor. Adding \"bean-shaped characters, noodle arms, flat digital colors\" reinforces the specific register.\n\n**Verification status:** VERIFIED in image generators. THEORETICAL for video (these styles should translate well to video generation since the visual simplicity and flat color approach is easier for models to maintain frame-to-frame).\n\n---\n\n### 1E. Genndy Tartakovsky / Action Cartoon (1990s-2020s)\n\n**Key exemplars:** Samurai Jack, Dexter's Laboratory, Powerpuff Girls, Primal\n\n**Visual characteristics:**\n- Evolution from thick bold outlines (Powerpuff Girls, Dexter's Lab) to no bold lines (Samurai Jack)\n- Extremely angular, geometric character design -- sharp silhouettes, blocky shapes\n- Dramatically limited color palettes (Samurai Jack used primarily black, white, green, and red)\n- Strong compositional framing inspired by Japanese woodblock prints and anime\n- Visual storytelling prioritized over dialogue\n\n**Motion characteristics:**\n- \"Pushed posing\" -- extreme, dramatic held poses\n- Sharp, snappy transitions between poses (not fluid arcs)\n- Rhythmic, musical timing -- every cut, gesture, and eye blink precisely timed\n- Action-dominated pacing (inverting the typical 20-minutes-dialogue/2-minutes-action ratio)\n\n**Prompt applicability:** MEDIUM. \"Samurai Jack style\" would be the most reliable named reference. Descriptor chain: \"angular geometric cartoon, dramatic silhouettes, limited color palette, strong compositions, action-oriented animation, minimal dialogue.\" The visual distinctiveness is high enough that models should differentiate it from generic cartoon, but the specific motion qualities (pushed posing, rhythmic timing) are not achievable through text prompting alone.\n\n**Verification status:** THEORETICAL for both image and video generation.\n\n---\n\n### 1F. Adult Western Animation (2020s premium)\n\n**Key exemplars:** Arcane, Castlevania, Invincible\n\n**Visual characteristics:**\n- **Arcane:** Hybrid 2D/3D -- 3D models with hand-painted textures creating a painterly, moving-painting aesthetic. Art Nouveau and Art Deco influences. Every frame is designed to stand alone as art.\n- **Castlevania:** Heavily anime-influenced (Satoshi Kon, Cowboy Bebop, Berserk references). Dark, detailed, and action-focused.\n- **Invincible:** Cel-shaded animation simulating shadows and highlights without fully transitioning to 3D. Graphic novel aesthetic with flat but dimensional feel. More violent and visceral than typical cartoon.\n\n**Motion characteristics:**\n- Smoother than TV animation, approaching theatrical quality\n- Castlevania uses anime-influenced motion (limited but impactful action cuts)\n- Invincible uses fluid motion during action but economical movement elsewhere\n- Arcane blends CG fluidity with hand-painted 2D effects\n\n**Prompt applicability:** MEDIUM. These styles are more complex and harder to trigger through text alone. \"Painterly animation style\" can approximate Arcane. \"Cel-shaded animation, graphic novel style, dramatic lighting\" can approach Invincible. Castlevania sits in an anime-adjacent space that may drift toward anime outputs. Reference images are strongly recommended for these styles.\n\n**Verification status:** THEORETICAL. These premium styles are the hardest to achieve through text-to-video prompting because they involve hybrid techniques that AI models may not cleanly reproduce.\n\n---\n\n### 1G. Flat Design / Motion Graphics (2010s-present)\n\n**Key exemplars:** Kurzgesagt, explainer videos, infographic animations\n\n**Visual characteristics:**\n- Flat 2D art with no realistic shading\n- Bright neon color palettes (pinks, blues, oranges) with strong contrast\n- Geometric shapes and clean vector lines\n- Rounded character designs with minimal detail\n- Gradient shading used sparingly for dimension (distinguishing from pure flat)\n- Infographic-style visual metaphors and data visualization\n\n**Motion characteristics:**\n- Smooth, mathematical motion (easing curves, not organic animation)\n- Objects morph, scale, and transition rather than move naturalistically\n- Highly choreographed -- motion serves information delivery\n- Camera movement is rare; objects move within frame\n\n**Prompt applicability:** HIGH. \"Kurzgesagt style\" is a recognized AI prompt keyword. \"Flat design animation, bright neon colors, geometric shapes, vector illustration, clean minimalist style, infographic animation\" produces consistent results. This style translates well to AI generation because its visual simplicity reduces the complexity the model must maintain.\n\n**Verification status:** VERIFIED in image generators (Midlibrary documents Kurzgesagt as a Midjourney-recognized style). THEORETICAL for video, but the visual simplicity is a strong predictor of success.\n\n---\n\n### 1H. Spider-Verse Hybrid (2018-present)\n\n**Key exemplars:** Spider-Man: Into the Spider-Verse, Across the Spider-Verse\n\n**Visual characteristics:**\n- 3D CGI with 2D hand-drawn overlays (outlines, effects, speed lines)\n- Visible Ben-Day dots (comic book printing texture)\n- Deliberate \"imperfections\" mimicking mass-production printing (color misalignment, density shifts)\n- Frame rate mixing (animation \"on twos\" for stylistic rawness vs. smooth motion for confidence)\n- Hand-drawn 2D effects layered on 3D simulations\n\n**Motion characteristics:**\n- Intentional frame rate manipulation as character development tool\n- 2D hand-drawn effects (explosions, fire) animated on twos alongside smooth 3D\n- Comic-book panel compositions in motion\n\n**Prompt applicability:** LOW-MEDIUM. This is a hybrid style that current AI video models struggle to replicate cleanly. \"Comic book animation style, Ben-Day dots, visible halftone, mixed frame rates\" captures some elements. The frame-rate mixing and 2D/3D overlay are not achievable through text prompting. A reference image approach (generating a Spider-Verse-style frame first, then animating it) would be more reliable.\n\n**Verification status:** THEORETICAL. This is one of the most technically complex cartoon styles, and AI video models are unlikely to produce it reliably from text alone.\n\n---\n\n## 2. CARTOON-SPECIFIC DESIGN PRINCIPLES\n\n### 2A. Exaggeration Spectrum\n\nCartoons occupy a fundamentally different position on the exaggeration spectrum than anime or 3D:\n\n| Dimension | Western Cartoon | Anime | Pixar/3D |\n|-----------|----------------|-------|----------|\n| Physical deformation | Extreme (characters flatten, shatter, stretch beyond recognition) | Moderate (primarily comedic gags, not realistic scenes) | Controlled (maintains believable physics) |\n| Facial expression | Impossible (eyes exit skull, jaw detaches) | Stylized (sweat drops, cross-veins, simplified emotion symbols) | Exaggerated but anatomically plausible |\n| Emotional expression | Physical impossibility IS the emotion | Symbolic shorthand (emotion icons) | Subtle facial animation |\n| Movement | Breaks all physics for comedic or dramatic effect | Impressionistic -- distortion only in gags, never \"realistic\" scenes | Respects physics, exaggerates within natural bounds |\n\n**Prompt applicability:** MEDIUM. You can prompt for \"extreme cartoon exaggeration\" or \"exaggerated squash and stretch\" but AI video models trained on realistic physics fundamentally struggle with cartoon physics. The deformations that define cartoon expression (eyes popping, body shattering, accordion-folding) require the model to violate its learned physical priors. This is a core limitation.\n\n**Verification status:** THEORETICAL. Research indicates that \"exaggerated dynamics often seen in anime do not always conform to strict physical laws\" and this \"presents a fundamental challenge for AI video models trained on realistic physics.\" This applies even more strongly to Western cartoon exaggeration, which is far more extreme than anime's.\n\n---\n\n### 2B. Line Weight and Outline Conventions\n\nCartoon outlines serve three functions:\n1. **Separation:** Thick lines frame characters against backgrounds, making shapes \"read\" clearly\n2. **Depth cue:** Thicker lines for foreground objects, thinner for background\n3. **Style register signal:** Line weight is the strongest visual cue for which cartoon sub-register is being used\n\n**Line weight taxonomy:**\n- **Thick-line (1950s-1970s):** Heavy black borders. UPA, Powerpuff Girls, Dexter's Lab. Strong, graphical, bold.\n- **Thin-line (2010s-present):** Subtle outlines. Adventure Time, Steven Universe, Gravity Falls. Soft, rounded, modern.\n- **Variable-weight (Golden Age):** Lines that taper and swell. Classic Disney, Warner Bros. Organic, hand-crafted feel.\n- **No outlines (rare):** Samurai Jack's later evolution. Shapes defined by color contrast alone.\n\n**Prompt applicability:** HIGH. \"Bold black outlines,\" \"thick outlines,\" \"thin clean lines,\" and \"no outlines, color-defined shapes\" are reliable style descriptors that AI models respond to consistently. These are among the most effective ways to steer cartoon sub-register.\n\n**Verification status:** VERIFIED in image generators. Line weight descriptors produce predictable differences in output. THEORETICAL for maintaining consistency in video (line weight may vary frame-to-frame).\n\n---\n\n### 2C. Color Approaches\n\n**Flat color (dominant cartoon convention):**\n- Solid, even colors without blending or smooth gradients\n- Shadows rendered as discrete color steps (not gradient ramps)\n- 3-5 primary colors per character (industry standard)\n- Bright, vivid, bold colors that \"attract the eye and supplement the energy of the design\"\n\n**Cel-shading (stylized depth):**\n- Step-like shadow transitions (crisp edges between light and shadow)\n- Limited tonal range (typically 2-3 tones per color: base, shadow, highlight)\n- High contrast between tones\n- Preserves flat-looking aesthetic while adding dimensionality\n\n**Gradient / painterly (premium styles):**\n- Smooth color transitions for atmosphere (Arcane, some modern TV shows)\n- More realistic but still stylized\n- Reserved for higher-budget productions\n\n**Prompt applicability:** HIGH. \"Flat colors, no gradients, solid color fills\" is extremely effective for locking cartoon register. \"Cel-shaded, flat shading, bold primary colors\" consistently produces cartoon-appropriate color treatment. This is one of the strongest levers for preventing drift to 3D or photorealism.\n\n**Verification status:** VERIFIED. Color descriptors are among the most reliable style controls in both image and video AI models.\n\n---\n\n### 2D. Shape Language\n\nCartoon character design is built on deliberate geometric foundations:\n\n- **Circles** = friendly, safe, approachable (Mickey Mouse: three circles)\n- **Squares** = stable, strong, reliable (Sulley from Monsters Inc.)\n- **Triangles** = dangerous, dynamic, unpredictable (Scar from The Lion King)\n\nCartoon design uses more extreme shape language than anime or 3D. Characters in Western cartoons are often reducible to 2-3 basic shapes. This creates strong silhouette readability -- a well-designed cartoon character is recognizable as a black silhouette.\n\n**Prompt applicability:** MEDIUM. You cannot directly prompt for \"circle-based character design\" and expect the model to understand shape language. But you can describe the result: \"round, soft character with large head and small body\" effectively produces circle-based designs. \"Angular, sharp character design\" produces triangle-based results.\n\n**Verification status:** VERIFIED in image generators. Shape descriptors in prompts produce predictable design outcomes.\n\n---\n\n## 3. CARTOON MOTION PRINCIPLES\n\n### 3A. Timing: Snap vs. Ease\n\n**Cartoon timing is fundamentally different from realistic or anime timing:**\n\n- **Snap-to-pose:** The Tex Avery signature. Character moves at maximum speed between poses, then holds. No gradual acceleration. The audience reads the pose, not the motion between poses. This is the opposite of realistic ease-in/ease-out.\n- **Moving holds:** A character arrives at a pose and mostly stops, but with subtle breathing or swaying to prevent \"death\" (looking frozen). Tex Avery would hold certain poses for just enough frames for the audience to read them.\n- **Beat-based timing:** Friz Freleng and Genndy Tartakovsky both approached animation timing musically, with gags landing on rhythmic beats.\n\n**How this differs:**\n| Style | Primary timing approach |\n|-------|----------------------|\n| Western cartoon | Snap-to-pose with holds |\n| Anime | Limited poses with \"sakuga\" bursts of fluid motion |\n| Pixar/3D | Smooth ease-in/ease-out throughout |\n| Motion graphics | Mathematical easing curves |\n\n**Prompt applicability:** LOW. AI video models do not offer frame-level timing control. You cannot prompt for \"snap-to-pose timing\" or \"held poses.\" The model will apply its default temporal interpolation. This is the single biggest gap between what makes cartoon motion feel like cartoon motion and what AI video models can produce.\n\n**Verification status:** THEORETICAL. No evidence that any current AI video model can produce snap-to-pose timing from text prompts. This represents a fundamental architectural limitation -- models are trained to produce smooth temporal coherence, which is the opposite of cartoon timing.\n\n---\n\n### 3B. Anticipation and Follow-Through\n\nIn Western cartoons, anticipation is massively exaggerated compared to other animation styles:\n- A character about to run stretches backward first (the bigger the stretch, the funnier)\n- The Coyote's split-second of realization before falling is a masterclass in anticipation timing\n- A punch winds up with the entire body, not just the arm\n\nFollow-through is equally extreme:\n- A character stops but their ears, tail, clothing keep moving\n- After a fast run, a character slides to a stop, body parts arriving at different times\n- Hair and clothing overshoot the final pose\n\n**Prompt applicability:** LOW-MEDIUM. You can describe \"exaggerated anticipation before action\" in prompts, and some AI models will produce a slight pause or wind-up before movement. But the extreme cartoon convention (body stretching backward before running forward) requires physical deformation that AI video models struggle with. \"Follow-through\" is slightly more achievable because it involves trailing elements, which models handle better.\n\n**Verification status:** THEORETICAL for the cartoon-specific extreme versions. General anticipation and follow-through are somewhat present in AI video output but not at cartoon exaggeration levels.\n\n---\n\n### 3C. \"Takes\" and \"Double Takes\"\n\nThe \"take\" is arguably the most cartoon-specific acting beat:\n- **Standard take:** Character reacts to stimulus with exaggerated whole-body response (eyes bulge, body stiffens, sometimes leaves the ground)\n- **Wild take (Tex Avery):** Eyes physically exit skull, jaw drops to floor, body parts disassemble and reassemble\n- **Double take:** Character looks, looks away, then SNAPS back with full take reaction\n- **Slow burn:** Gradual realization building to explosive reaction (associated with Edgar Kennedy in live action, adapted extensively in cartoons)\n\nThese are unique to the cartoon register. Anime uses symbolic reaction shots (sweat drops, cross-veins). Pixar uses subtle facial animation. Only Western cartoons use physical impossibility as the primary emotional expression mechanism.\n\n**Prompt applicability:** LOW. \"Character does a surprised take\" in a prompt will likely produce a subtle reaction, not the cartoon convention. The physical impossibility elements (eyes leaving sockets, jaw stretching to floor) are extremely unlikely to be produced by current AI video models because they violate the physical priors the models have learned. This is one of the defining features of cartoon animation that AI cannot currently replicate.\n\n**Verification status:** THEORETICAL. No evidence of AI video models producing authentic cartoon takes from text prompts.\n\n---\n\n### 3D. Smear Frames and Stretch Frames\n\nSmear frames are individual frames where a character is stretched, duplicated, or distorted to create the illusion of speed. They look wrong when paused but create fluid motion when played at speed.\n\nTypes:\n- **Elongated smear:** Character stretched along the path of motion\n- **Multiple smear:** Character appears in several positions simultaneously within one frame\n- **Speed lines:** Abstract lines indicating direction and velocity of motion\n- **Motion arcs:** Curved paths that limbs and objects follow during fast movement\n\n**Prompt applicability:** LOW for actual smear frames (AI models try to make every frame look \"correct,\" which is antithetical to smear frame logic). MEDIUM for speed lines (can be prompted as \"cartoon speed lines\" or \"motion lines\" and AI models will sometimes add them). Motion arcs are somewhat naturally present in AI-generated motion.\n\n**Verification status:** Speed lines: VERIFIED in image generation. Smear frames: THEORETICAL -- these require the model to deliberately produce \"incorrect\" single frames that work in sequence, which conflicts with per-frame optimization approaches.\n\n---\n\n### 3E. Cartoon Physics / \"Toon Physics\"\n\nWalt Disney called this \"The Impossible Plausible.\" Key conventions:\n\n- **Squashed flat:** Character hit by heavy object becomes paper-thin, then pops back\n- **Accordion fold:** Character compressed into pleated shape\n- **Gravity delay:** Character runs off cliff, hangs in air, looks down, THEN falls\n- **Shatter/reassemble:** Character breaks into pieces, then reforms\n- **Elastic body:** Limbs stretch to impossible lengths, body inflates/deflates\n- **Object interaction:** Characters can reach into painted holes, pull objects from behind their back, produce items from nowhere\n\n**Prompt applicability:** LOW. These require the AI model to violate physical laws in specific, deliberate ways. Current models are trained toward physical plausibility. The gravity delay (running off a cliff and pausing) might be partially achievable through careful prompting, but deliberate physical impossibility (shattering and reforming) is not.\n\n**Verification status:** THEORETICAL. Research paper PhysAnimator (2025) specifically addresses the challenge that \"exaggerated dynamics often seen in [cartoon animation] do not always conform to strict physical laws\" and proposes physics-guided approaches to solve this, confirming it as an unsolved problem in current models.\n\n---\n\n## 4. VEO/AI VIDEO MODEL BEHAVIOR WITH CARTOON STYLES\n\n### 4A. What Reliably Triggers Cartoon (Not Anime, Not 3D)\n\n**Effective descriptors (ranked by reliability):**\n\n1. **\"2D cartoon animation, flat colors, bold outlines\"** -- The most reliable combo for Western cartoon output. VERIFIED across community reports.\n\n2. **\"Cel-shaded animation\"** -- Produces a flat, outlined look. Can drift toward anime cel-shading if not anchored with Western descriptors. HIGH reliability.\n\n3. **\"Hand-drawn animation style\"** -- Triggers 2D aesthetic but can be ambiguous (hand-drawn anime exists). Add \"Western cartoon\" or \"Cartoon Network\" to anchor.\n\n4. **Named studio/show references** -- \"Hanna-Barbera style,\" \"in the style of Looney Tunes\" (when not filtered). MEDIUM reliability (IP filtering may block some references).\n\n5. **Period descriptors** -- \"1940s theatrical cartoon,\" \"1990s Cartoon Network style,\" \"2010s thin-line animation.\" MEDIUM reliability.\n\n6. **\"Cartoon\"** alone -- Produces unpredictable results. May drift to 3D, anime, or photorealistic cartoon. LOW reliability as a standalone keyword.\n\n**Key finding:** Fewer, more specific style tokens consistently outperform longer, vaguer descriptions. \"More style words leads to 'style soup' and unpredictable results; if your style line is 30 words long, the model will average them together unpredictably.\" Pick one precise style anchor and support it with 2-3 reinforcing descriptors.\n\n**Verification status:** MIXED. Descriptor effectiveness is VERIFIED in image generators and community reports. Veo-specific behavior is PARTIALLY VERIFIED (official Veo guides list \"classic Disney animation style\" and \"cel-shaded animation\" as supported style keywords).\n\n---\n\n### 4B. Common Failure Modes\n\n1. **Drift to anime:** Happens when using \"animation\" without Western anchors. Any mention of \"expressive eyes\" or \"colorful hair\" accelerates anime drift. **Mitigation:** Lead with \"Western cartoon\" or \"American animation style.\"\n\n2. **Drift to 3D/Pixar:** Happens when using \"cartoon\" alone or with quality descriptors like \"detailed\" or \"high quality.\" The model's default for \"high quality cartoon\" trends toward 3D rendering. **Mitigation:** Explicitly include \"flat colors, no 3D rendering, 2D\" and avoid terms like \"rendered\" or \"detailed shading.\"\n\n3. **Style inconsistency across frames:** Flat-shaded styles can exhibit \"inevitable drift\" where some frames gain unwanted shading or depth. **Mitigation:** Use reference images. Tools like WAN 2.2 reportedly handle flat-shaded styles better than Kling for this reason.\n\n4. **Attribute bleed:** Style descriptors meant for the overall aesthetic can \"bleed\" onto specific characters, causing all characters to share the same accessories or features. **Mitigation:** Separate character descriptions from style descriptions in prompts.\n\n5. **Compression artifact amplification:** When using image-to-video workflows, compression artifacts in the reference image are amplified across every frame. **Mitigation:** Ensure reference images are clean, high-resolution, and free of compression blocks.\n\n**Verification status:** VERIFIED from community reports and tool-specific documentation.\n\n---\n\n### 4C. Negative Prompt Strategy\n\nFor maintaining cartoon register, the most effective negative prompt elements (where supported):\n\n- \"NOT photorealistic, NOT 3D rendering, NOT anime\"\n- \"No gradients, no realistic shading, no volumetric lighting\"\n- \"Avoid depth of field, avoid lens effects, avoid film grain\" (these trigger photorealistic processing)\n\n**Important caveat:** Veo 3.1 does not support explicit negative prompts in the same way as Stable Diffusion. Style control in Veo is achieved through positive descriptors and reference images, not exclusions. For Veo, the strategy is to be maximally specific about what you WANT rather than what you don't want.\n\n**Prompt applicability:** HIGH for models that support negative prompts (Stable Diffusion, some image generators). MEDIUM for Veo (use positive-only strategy).\n\n**Verification status:** VERIFIED for image generators. THEORETICAL for Veo-specific negative prompt handling.\n\n---\n\n### 4D. Reference Image Workflow (Recommended for Cartoon)\n\nThe most reliable approach for cartoon style in Veo 3.1:\n\n1. Generate a reference image in your target cartoon style using an image generator (Midjourney, Gemini Flash, etc.)\n2. Ensure the reference image has clean lines, no compression artifacts, and the exact style you want preserved\n3. Feed the reference image to Veo 3.1 with a text prompt that describes the desired motion\n4. Optionally add \"maintain the style of the input image\" in the text prompt\n5. Use up to 3 reference images for multi-character scenes\n\n\"The quality of style preservation in Veo 3 is remarkable -- from cartoon aesthetics to photographic color grading, the model maintains the visual characteristics that make your original image unique.\"\n\n**Prompt applicability:** HIGH. This is the single most effective technique for reliable cartoon style output.\n\n**Verification status:** VERIFIED from multiple community sources and official documentation.\n\n---\n\n## 5. CARTOON CHARACTER EXPRESSIVENESS IN AI\n\n### 5A. What Translates to AI Video Prompting\n\n| Expression technique | Cartoon convention | AI achievability | Notes |\n|---------------------|-------------------|-----------------|-------|\n| Exaggerated smile/frown | Wide mouth, extreme curves | HIGH | Models handle basic facial expression exaggeration |\n| Blushing/color change | Color shift on cheeks/face | MEDIUM | Sometimes achieved, not always consistent |\n| Sweat drops / emotion symbols | Floating visual indicators | MEDIUM | More reliably achieved in anime style than Western cartoon |\n| Body language amplification | Character leans/shrinks/puffs up | MEDIUM | Moderate exaggeration achievable, extreme not |\n| Eye scaling | Eyes growing/shrinking for emotion | MEDIUM | Subtle scaling works; extreme (eyes popping out) does not |\n| Speed/excitement vibration | Character vibrates or blurs with excitement | LOW-MEDIUM | Some AI models produce a version of this |\n| Wild take (eyes exit head) | Physical impossibility | LOW | Violates learned physical priors |\n| Jaw drop to floor | Physical impossibility | LOW | Same limitation |\n| Body shattering/reforming | Physical impossibility | LOW | Same limitation |\n| Accordion fold after impact | Physical impossibility | LOW | Same limitation |\n| Gravity delay (cliff edge) | Temporal impossibility | LOW | Models default to continuous physics |\n\n### 5B. The Core Limitation\n\nThe fundamental barrier is that cartoon expressiveness depends heavily on physical impossibility -- and AI video models are optimized for physical plausibility. The \"take\" (the primary emotional beat in cartoon acting) requires the character's body to do something that would be physically impossible, and this is precisely what current models are trained NOT to do.\n\n**Practical recommendation:** For AI cartoon video, lean into expression techniques that work (exaggerated facial expressions, amplified body language, color-based emotion indicators) and avoid prompting for techniques that require physical impossibility (takes, jaw drops, eye pops, shattering). Design your cartoon content around what the tools can do, not what traditional cartoons do.\n\n**Verification status:** THEORETICAL but strongly supported by the research finding that AI models trained on realistic physics struggle with cartoon dynamics, confirmed by the PhysAnimator research paper (2025).\n\n---\n\n## 6. ACTIONABLE PROMPT TEMPLATES\n\n### Template A: Golden Age Theatrical Cartoon\n```\nA [character description] in classic 1940s theatrical cartoon animation style.\nBold black outlines, saturated flat colors, exaggerated proportions.\n[Action description]. Hand-painted background, warm color palette.\n```\n\n### Template B: Rubber Hose / Vintage\n```\nA [character description] in 1930s rubber hose animation style.\nPie-eyed character, white gloves, round shapes, no jointed limbs.\n[Action description]. Black and white with slight film grain, bouncy rhythmic movement.\n```\n\n### Template C: Modern TV Cartoon (CalArts-adjacent)\n```\nA [character description] in modern Western cartoon style.\nThin clean lines, round shapes, bright saturated colors, flat digital coloring.\n[Action description]. Simple clean background, Cartoon Network aesthetic.\n```\n\n### Template D: Flat Design / Kurzgesagt\n```\nA [character description] in Kurzgesagt flat design animation style.\nGeometric shapes, bright neon color palette, clean vector lines, minimal detail.\n[Action description]. Infographic-style visual, smooth mathematical motion.\n```\n\n### Template E: Action Cartoon\n```\nA [character description] in dramatic action cartoon style.\nAngular geometric design, limited color palette, strong silhouettes, bold compositions.\n[Action description]. Minimal background detail, high contrast, dynamic poses.\n```\n\n### Template F: Adult Animation (Graphic Novel)\n```\nA [character description] in graphic novel animation style.\nCel-shaded, dramatic lighting, detailed character design, mature aesthetic.\n[Action"}],"stop_reason":null,"stop_sequence":null,"stop_details":null,"usage":{"input_tokens":1,"cache_creation_input_tokens":917,"cache_read_input_tokens":60714,"cache_creation":{"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":917,"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":0},"output_tokens":6,"service_tier":"standard","inference_geo":"not_available"}},"requestId":"req_011CZniszJk3LFxZqM1hXWzS","type":"assistant","uuid":"47f6d5bc-9d76-4910-9f49-443edb4b7947","timestamp":"2026-04-06T15:07:47.283Z","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/root","sessionId":"00431266-ecaa-4922-87fe-ffcae2b0b919","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"main","slug":"misty-purring-bentley"}

Let me compile this into the final research document.\n\n---\n\n# Cartoon Animation Styles for AI Video Prompting: Research Document\n\n## Purpose\n\nMap the Western 2D cartoon style landscape (non-anime, non-3D-Pixar) as it applies to AI video generation prompting, specifically Veo 3.1 and comparable text-to-video models.\n\n---\n\n## 1. Cartoon Sub-Register Taxonomy\n\n### 1A. Classic Theatrical (1930s-1960s Golden Age)\n\n**Shows/Studios:** Looney Tunes, Tom & Jerry, early Disney shorts, MGM cartoons, Tex Avery\n\n**Visual Characteristics:**\n- Full animation at 24fps (every frame drawn), though some used \"on twos\" (12 drawings/sec)\n- Thick black outlines around characters and objects\n- Flat color fills with limited shading (cel painting: colors on back, outlines on front)\n- Extreme squash-and-stretch with volume preservation\n- Characters have strong silhouettes and readability\n- Backgrounds painted in watercolor or gouache, often more detailed than characters\n- High exaggeration: eyes popping from sockets, jaw-dropping to floor, accordion-body after impacts\n- \"Wild takes\" (Tex Avery's signature): lightning-fast action snapping to held extreme poses\n\n**Prompt Language for AI:**\n- `\"classic cartoon animation style, golden age of American animation, thick black outlines, flat cel colors, squash and stretch, exaggerated expressions\"` -- **HIGH applicability, THEORETICAL** (from animation principles; no published Veo-specific A/B tests for this exact phrasing)\n- `\"Looney Tunes style, hand-drawn 2D animation, bold outlines, vibrant flat colors, slapstick comedy, exaggerated motion\"` -- **HIGH applicability, THEORETICAL**\n- `\"in the style of Tex Avery cartoon, wild take, snappy poses, extreme exaggeration\"` -- **MEDIUM applicability, THEORETICAL** (named artist references work in image models; video model behavior less documented)\n\n**What evidence would disprove this mapping:** If Veo 3.1 collapses all \"classic cartoon\" descriptors into a single generic output regardless of specificity, the sub-distinctions would be meaningless for prompting.\n\n---\n\n### 1B. Retro/Vintage (1920s-1930s Pre-Code)\n\n**Shows/Studios:** Fleischer Studios (Betty Boop, Popeye, Out of the Inkwell), early Mickey Mouse, Felix the Cat, Cuphead (modern recreation)\n\n**Visual Characteristics:**\n- **Rubber hose limbs:** Arms and legs drawn as flowing curves without articulation -- no hinged elbows or knees\n- **Pie eyes:** Large round eyes, often with a pie-slice cut for pupils\n- **Circular geometry:** Round heads, circular hands (often with white gloves), oversized shoes\n- **High-contrast B&W or limited color:** Heavy black ink on white backgrounds; when color exists, very limited palette\n- **Surreal/subversive content:** Inanimate objects come alive, environments shift and morph\n- **Rhythmic bounce:** Characters bob and sway constantly, often synchronized to music\n- **Line weight variation frame-to-frame:** Imperfect, organic feel (Cuphead deliberately recreated this)\n- **Film grain and imperfection:** Dust, scratches, slight blur (authentic to era)\n\n**Prompt Language for AI:**\n- `\"1930s rubber hose animation style, pie eyes, white gloves, round shapes, flowing limbs without joints, black and white, vintage cartoon\"` -- **HIGH applicability, THEORETICAL**\n- `\"Fleischer Studios style animation, surreal cartoon, bouncing rhythm, high-contrast black and white, vintage film grain\"` -- **MEDIUM applicability, THEORETICAL** (studio name may or may not be in training data)\n- `\"Cuphead art style, hand-drawn 1930s cartoon aesthetic, watercolor backgrounds, rubber hose characters, film grain\"` -- **HIGH applicability, THEORETICAL** (Cuphead is well-known enough to likely exist in training data)\n\n---\n\n### 1C. Limited Animation / UPA Style (1950s-1970s)\n\n**Shows/Studios:** UPA (Gerald McBoing-Boing, Mr. Magoo), early Hanna-Barbera (Flintstones, Jetsons, Scooby-Doo), Jay Ward (Rocky & Bullwinkle)\n\n**Visual Characteristics:**\n- **Flat, graphic shapes** rather than rounded Disney naturalism -- characters as icons, not figures\n- **Minimal movement:** Only parts of the frame that need to move are redrawn (8-12 fps effective)\n- **Abstract/minimalist backgrounds:** Sometimes disappearing to a few clean lines atop bold flat colors\n- **Thick outer outlines, thin interior lines** (Hanna-Barbera signature)\n- **Strong silhouettes and geometric character design**\n- **Static poses with maximum personality** -- charm from design, not motion\n- **Bold, flat color palettes** with no gradients\n\n**Prompt Language for AI:**\n- `\"Hanna-Barbera animation style, 1960s cartoon, bold outlines, flat colors, geometric character design, limited animation, retro TV cartoon\"` -- **HIGH applicability, VERIFIED** (Midlibrary confirms Hanna-Barbera produces recognizable outputs in image models)\n- `\"UPA style animation, flat graphic design, minimal backgrounds, abstract shapes, bold colors, mid-century modern cartoon\"` -- **LOW applicability, THEORETICAL** (UPA is obscure enough that it may not trigger reliably in AI models)\n- `\"retro Saturday morning cartoon, simplified characters, flat colors, thick black outlines\"` -- **MEDIUM applicability, THEORETICAL**\n\n---\n\n### 1D. Modern TV Animation / \"CalArts\" Thin-Line (2010s-present)\n\n**Shows/Studios:** Adventure Time, Steven Universe, Gravity Falls, Star vs. the Forces of Evil, The Owl House, Craig of the Creek\n\n**Visual Characteristics:**\n- **Thin lines** (vs. thick lines of prior eras) -- emerged from Flash/digital animation tools\n- **Round shapes, \"bean-shaped\" heads, \"bean mouths\"**\n- **Noodle arms** (thin, flexible limbs without defined muscle)\n- **Big or tiny dot eyes** with simple line mouths\n- **Bright, saturated flat colors** with minimal shading\n- **Simplified character designs** readable at small sizes and in silhouette\n- **Characters remain recognizable even highly deformed** during action\n\n**Prompt Language for AI:**\n- `\"modern Cartoon Network animation style, thin lines, round shapes, bright flat colors, simplified character design, 2D digital animation\"` -- **HIGH applicability, THEORETICAL**\n- `\"Adventure Time animation style, simple geometric characters, bright colors, thin outlines, whimsical\"` -- **HIGH applicability, THEORETICAL** (well-known show, likely in training data)\n- `\"Steven Universe animation style, pastel colors, expressive eyes, soft rounded characters\"` -- **HIGH applicability, THEORETICAL**\n\n---\n\n### 1E. Tartakovsky / Cartoon Network Action Style (late 1990s-2000s)\n\n**Shows/Studios:** Samurai Jack, Powerpuff Girls, Dexter's Laboratory, Clone Wars (2003)\n\n**Visual Characteristics:**\n- **Pushed posing:** Characters hold extreme dynamic poses, transitioning sharply between them\n- **Sharp angular silhouettes** and blocky shapes (Dexter's Lab, Powerpuff Girls: thick outlines)\n- **Samurai Jack's evolution:** No thick outlines, solid colors, bold silhouettes, limited palette (black, white, red, green), heavy Japanese woodblock print (Ukiyo-e) influence\n- **Minimal dialogue, maximum visual storytelling**\n- **Strong contrast and dramatic compositions** -- every frame could be a poster\n- **Rhythmic, musical timing** -- cuts, gestures, eye blinks all precisely timed to beats\n\n**Prompt Language for AI:**\n- `\"Samurai Jack animation style, bold silhouettes, limited color palette, dramatic composition, stylized action, minimal detail, strong contrast\"` -- **MEDIUM applicability, THEORETICAL** (distinctive enough to be in training data, but the visual language is very specific)\n- `\"Powerpuff Girls animation style, thick outlines, blocky geometric characters, bright colors, dynamic action poses\"` -- **HIGH applicability, THEORETICAL**\n- `\"Genndy Tartakovsky animation style, sharp angular characters, pushed poses, dramatic composition\"` -- **LOW applicability, THEORETICAL** (animator name may not trigger reliably)\n\n---\n\n### 1F. Adult Western Animation (2020s Renaissance)\n\n**Shows/Studios:** Arcane, Castlevania, Invincible, Primal\n\n**Visual Characteristics:**\n\n**Arcane:**\n- Hybrid 2D/3D: 3D models with hand-painted textures creating painterly aesthetic\n- 2D elements for effects (smoke, water)\n- Art Nouveau and Art Deco visual influences\n- Every frame designed as standalone artwork\n- NOT flat 2D -- this is a category overlap with 3D\n\n**Castlevania:**\n- Heavily anime-influenced (Satoshi Kon, Cowboy Bebop, Berserk)\n- Western content with Japanese animation visual language\n- More detailed shading and cross-hatching than typical Western cartoon\n- NOT purely Western cartoon -- this is anime-adjacent\n\n**Invincible:**\n- Cel-shaded animation: flat appearance with simulated shadows/highlights\n- Stays true to comic book/graphic novel origins\n- Fluid action motion while maintaining flat aesthetic\n- Bold outlines, saturated colors, clean character design\n\n**Primal (Tartakovsky):**\n- Zero dialogue\n- Extreme violence with stylized animation\n- Loose, expressive line work\n- Color as emotional storytelling tool\n\n**Prompt Language for AI:**\n- `\"Invincible comic book animation style, cel-shaded, bold outlines, flat colors with dramatic shadows, action scene\"` -- **MEDIUM applicability, THEORETICAL**\n- `\"adult Western animation, graphic novel aesthetic, cel-shaded, detailed character design, dramatic lighting\"` -- **MEDIUM applicability, THEORETICAL**\n\n**Important caveat:** Arcane and Castlevania are NOT clean \"cartoon\" sub-registers. Arcane is a 3D/2D hybrid closer to the Pixar register, and Castlevania is functionally anime made in the West. Including them under \"cartoon\" is a taxonomic error -- they belong to adjacent registers.\n\n---\n\n### 1G. Flat Design / Motion Graphics (Explainer/Educational)\n\n**Shows/Studios:** Kurzgesagt, TED-Ed animations, explainer videos, Headspace\n\n**Visual Characteristics:**\n- **Flat 2D vector illustration** -- clean, mathematical precision\n- **Bright neon color palette** with contrasting hues (pinks, blues, oranges)\n- **Rounded shapes** and geometric simplicity\n- **Minimal to no outlines** (shape defined by color contrast, not lines)\n- **Gradient shading used sparingly** for depth, not realism\n- **Infographic integration** -- data visualization within animation\n- **Smooth, purposeful motion** -- objects transform, morph, and transition\n\n**Prompt Language for AI:**\n- `\"Kurzgesagt animation style, flat vector illustration, bright neon colors, geometric shapes, clean design, educational animation\"` -- **HIGH applicability, VERIFIED** (Midlibrary confirms Kurzgesagt as a recognized style in image generation)\n- `\"flat design motion graphics, vector art animation, bright contrasting colors, minimal outlines, smooth transitions\"` -- **HIGH applicability, THEORETICAL**\n- `\"explainer video animation style, isometric flat design, pastel colors, simple geometric characters\"` -- **HIGH applicability, THEORETICAL**\n\n---\n\n### 1H. Spider-Verse Hybrid (2018-present)\n\n**Visual Characteristics:**\n- 3D CGI with hand-drawn 2D overlays\n- Visible Ben-Day dots (comic print halftone)\n- Variable frame rates (Miles on twos = choppy; experienced heroes on ones = smooth)\n- Motion lines, comic-book impact effects, visible rendering imperfections\n- Color misalignment mimicking print production\n- Each universe has its own animation style (watercolor, futuristic, etc.)\n\n**Prompt Language for AI:**\n- `\"Spider-Verse animation style, comic book aesthetic, Ben-Day dots, mixed frame rates, motion lines, vibrant colors, hand-drawn effects over 3D\"` -- **MEDIUM applicability, THEORETICAL** (complex hybrid style may not translate cleanly to AI video; likely produces approximation)\n\n**Caveat:** This is technically a 3D-first hybrid, not pure 2D cartoon. Including it because the visual output reads as \"cartoon\" to audiences, and the prompt language is useful for understanding what AI models do with mixed-register instructions.\n\n---\n\n## 2. Cartoon-Specific Design Principles\n\n### 2A. Exaggeration Spectrum\n\n| Register | Exaggeration Level | How It Manifests |\n|----------|-------------------|-----------------|\n| **Pixar/3D** | Controlled | Proportions exaggerated in design, but movement stays physics-plausible. Squash-stretch present but subtle. |\n| **Anime** | Selective | Distortion reserved almost exclusively for comedic/symbolic gags. Realistic scenes maintain proportions. \"Impressionistic\" motion. |\n| **Western Cartoon (Classic)** | Extreme | Physical impossibility is the norm. Characters flatten, stretch, shatter, and reconstitute. Volume preservation is a *guideline*, not a rule. Eyes leave sockets. Jaw drops to floor. |\n| **Western Cartoon (Modern TV)** | Moderate | Characters stretch and deform but within narrower bounds than Golden Age. More \"bendy\" than \"explosive.\" |\n| **Flat/Motion Graphics** | Minimal | Shapes morph and transition smoothly. Exaggeration is in transformation, not distortion. |\n\n**Prompt Applicability: HIGH (conceptual framework) / THEORETICAL**\n\nWhen prompting for cartoon, you must specify the *degree* of exaggeration. \"Cartoon style\" alone gives the model no guidance on where in this spectrum to land. \"Extreme squash and stretch, Looney Tunes level exaggeration\" is more actionable than \"cartoon exaggeration.\"\n\n---\n\n### 2B. Line Weight and Outline Conventions\n\n**Taxonomy of line approaches in cartoon:**\n\n| Sub-Register | Line Weight | Line Character |\n|-------------|------------|----------------|\n| Golden Age Theatrical | Heavy, thick | Consistent weight, clean |\n| Rubber Hose (1920s-30s) | Medium, variable | Organic, wobbles frame-to-frame |\n| UPA/Limited (1950s-70s) | Thick outer, thin inner | Graphic, precise |\n| Hanna-Barbera TV | Thick, tapered | Bold outer contours, minimal interior lines |\n| CalArts/Modern TV | Thin, uniform | Clean, digital, noodle-like |\n| Tartakovsky (Samurai Jack) | None to minimal | Shape defined by color, not outline |\n| Flat/Motion Graphics | None | Shape defined by color blocks |\n| Adult (Invincible) | Medium, consistent | Comic book precision |\n\n**Prompt Applicability: HIGH / THEORETICAL**\n\nLine weight is one of the strongest visual signals for sub-register. Prompting with `\"thick black outlines\"` vs. `\"thin clean lines\"` vs. `\"no outlines, shapes defined by color\"` is a high-leverage differentiator.\n\n---\n\n### 2C. Color Approaches\n\n**Flat Color (Classic Cartoon Default):**\n- Solid, even colors without blending or smooth gradients\n- Limited palette: 3-5 primary colors per character\n- Bold, vivid, saturated hues\n- Shadows rendered as separate flat color shapes (not gradients)\n\n**Cel-Shading (Invincible, Some Modern):**\n- Flat color with step-function shadow/highlight (not smooth gradient)\n- Crisp boundaries between lit and shadow regions\n- Maintains \"flat\" read while adding depth\n\n**Gradient/Painterly (Arcane, Some Film):**\n- Smooth color transitions\n- Atmospheric depth\n- NOT characteristic of \"cartoon\" register -- this pushes toward illustration or 3D\n\n**Prompt applicability:**\n- `\"flat colors, no gradients, solid color fills\"` -- **HIGH / THEORETICAL** (directly controls color rendering approach)\n- `\"cel-shaded, step shadows, flat color with sharp shadow boundaries\"` -- **HIGH / VERIFIED** (cel-shaded is a recognized AI generation keyword per multiple prompt guides)\n- `\"limited color palette, 3-4 colors per character\"` -- **MEDIUM / THEORETICAL** (models may interpret loosely)\n\n---\n\n### 2D. Shape Language\n\nThe three core shapes drive cartoon character personality:\n- **Circles** = friendly, soft, safe (Mickey Mouse, Steven Universe)\n- **Squares** = stable, strong, dependable (Sulley, many hero builds)\n- **Triangles** = dangerous, dynamic, cunning (Scar, villain archetypes)\n\nModern Western cartoons lean heavily toward circles and rounded shapes (CalArts/thin-line era). Classic theatrical varied more widely. Tartakovsky used extreme angular/triangular language.\n\n**Prompt Applicability: MEDIUM / THEORETICAL** -- Shape language is more relevant for reference image creation (step 1 of image-to-video workflow) than for text-only video prompts. You can prompt `\"round, friendly character design\"` or `\"angular, sharp character design\"` but the model's interpretation varies.\n\n---\n\n### 2E. Rubber Hose Animation Conventions\n\nSpecific to the 1920s-30s and its modern revivals (Cuphead, indie games):\n- **No joints:** Limbs flow as continuous curves, no elbows or knees\n- **Constant rhythmic bounce:** Characters sway and bob even when \"idle\"\n- **Universal flexibility:** Everything bends -- characters, objects, backgrounds\n- **Circular design vocabulary:** Everything built from circles\n- **White gloves, oversized shoes** as near-universal character elements\n- **Pie-cut eyes** as the default expression system\n\n**Prompt Applicability: HIGH / THEORETICAL** -- These are specific, describable visual features that AI models can parse. `\"rubber hose animation, no joints, flowing limbs, pie eyes, white gloves, bouncing idle movement\"` provides concrete visual targets.\n\n---\n\n## 3. What Makes Cartoon Motion \"Feel\" Like a Cartoon?\n\n### 3A. Timing: Snap vs. Ease\n\n**Cartoon timing is defined by contrast between extremes:**\n- **Snap to pose:** Lightning-fast movement suddenly stopping on a held extreme pose (Tex Avery's signature)\n- **Moving holds:** Character reaches a pose and holds it, but with subtle breathing/swaying motion to prevent \"death\" feeling\n- **Anticipation-action-reaction cycle:** Much more exaggerated than realistic animation. A character about to run will compress dramatically before launching.\n\n**In contrast:**\n- Realistic/Pixar motion uses smooth ease-in/ease-out curves throughout\n- Anime uses sharp cuts between static held poses (limited animation tradition)\n- Cartoon snaps *between* poses and holds *at* poses\n\n**Prompt Applicability: LOW-MEDIUM / THEORETICAL**\n\nCurrent AI video models do not expose timing controls. You cannot specify \"snap to pose\" or \"ease-in duration.\" What you CAN do: describe the *outcome* of specific timing -- `\"character suddenly freezes in an exaggerated reaction pose\"` or `\"quick snappy movements with dramatic pauses\"`. Whether the model produces genuine cartoon timing vs. smooth interpolation is model-dependent.\n\n---\n\n### 3B. Anticipation and Follow-Through\n\n**In cartoon vs. other registers:**\n- **Cartoon:** A character about to jump compresses to 1/3 their height, pauses for a beat, then rockets upward stretching to 3x their height. Hair, clothes, and appendages trail behind, then overshoot the destination and settle back.\n- **Pixar:** Same principle but within 20% distortion range. Physics-plausible.\n- **Anime:** Anticipation shown through held static poses, speed lines, and camera cuts rather than body deformation.\n\nThe Wile E. Coyote/Road Runner shorts are the canonical masterclass: every gag is structured as anticipation (the plan), the pregnant pause (the realization), and the punchline (the failure).\n\n**Prompt Applicability: MEDIUM / THEORETICAL**\n\nDescribable as action sequences: `\"character winds up dramatically before throwing\"` or `\"character's body compresses before leaping, then stretches during the jump\"`. AI models can interpret these as motion descriptions, but whether they produce genuinely exaggerated cartoon anticipation or just normal motion is uncertain.\n\n---\n\n### 3C. The \"Take\" and \"Double Take\"\n\nThese are cartoon-specific acting conventions with no real equivalent in other animation registers:\n\n- **The Take:** Character reacts to shocking information with extreme physical distortion -- eyes bug out of head, jaw drops to floor, entire body levitates, skin tone changes, steam erupts from ears. Originated in classic theatrical shorts (Tex Avery perfected it).\n- **The Double Take:** Character initially ignores the stimulus, then snaps back with the full take reaction. The delay creates comedy.\n- **The Slow Burn:** Character's reaction builds incrementally from calm to explosive (less physical distortion, more progressive facial change).\n\n**Prompt Applicability: LOW / THEORETICAL**\n\nThis is the hardest cartoon-specific element for AI video. Describing `\"character does a wild cartoon take, eyes popping out of head in shock\"` requires the model to understand that this is a *convention* (a stylized expression system), not literal body horror. AI video models trained primarily on realistic physics and anatomy may struggle to produce \"eyes leaving sockets\" as comedy rather than grotesque error.\n\n**What would disprove this limitation:** If Veo 3.1 can produce recognizable cartoon takes from text-only prompts with cartoon style anchoring, it would indicate the model has learned this convention from animation training data.\n\n---\n\n### 3D. Smear Frames and Stretch Frames\n\n- **Smear frames:** Deliberately distorted frames that simulate motion blur by stretching the character's form along the motion path. A single frame might show a character's hand in 3 positions simultaneously.\n- **Motion arcs:** All movement follows curved paths, never straight lines (a principle in all animation but most visible/exaggerated in cartoons)\n- **Speed lines:** Graphic lines trailing behind fast-moving objects (shared with anime/manga)\n- **Multiples:** Character appendages shown in multiple positions within one frame to convey vibration or rapid motion\n\n**Prompt Applicability: LOW / THEORETICAL**\n\nSmear frames are *single-frame* distortions that rely on persistence of vision. AI video models generate frame-by-frame and are unlikely to produce deliberate single-frame distortions. Speed lines are more achievable: `\"speed lines trailing behind character, motion blur effect\"` -- **MEDIUM applicability**. The model may produce something motion-blur-like rather than genuine cartoon smear.\n\n---\n\n### 3E. AI-Achievable vs. AI-Unreachable Cartoon Motion\n\n**More achievable through prompting:**\n- Overall cartoon visual style (flat colors, bold outlines, simplified characters)\n- Exaggerated proportions and character design\n- Speed lines and basic motion trails\n- General \"bouncy\" or \"energetic\" motion quality\n- Broad physical comedy (character slipping, falling, running)\n\n**Less achievable / speculative:**\n- Frame-precise timing (snap-to-pose, held poses, beat drops)\n- Genuine smear frames (single-frame distortions)\n- Wild takes with physical impossibility (eyes leaving head, jaw hitting floor)\n- Rubber hose jointless limb deformation during motion\n- The specific rhythm of anticipation-action-reaction with correct beat timing\n- Squash and stretch with volume preservation during rapid motion\n\n**What would change this assessment:** If Veo 3.1 or successor models expose temporal control parameters (timing curves, keyframe hold durations) or if fine-tuning on cartoon animation datasets becomes available.\n\n---\n\n## 4. Veo/AI Video Model Behavior with Cartoon Styles\n\n### 4A. Style Descriptors That Work\n\nBased on community reports and official guides:\n\n**HIGH reliability (documented in official guides or widely tested):**\n- `\"cel-shaded animation\"` -- recognized keyword, produces flat-shaded stylized output\n- `\"classic Disney animation style\"` -- recognized, though may drift toward 3D Disney\n- `\"Japanese anime style\"` -- strong trigger but wrong register for cartoon\n- `\"Pixar-like 3D animation\"` -- strong trigger but wrong register\n- `\"claymation style\"` -- strong trigger, distinct aesthetic\n\n**MEDIUM reliability (community-reported, less formally tested):**\n- `\"2D hand-drawn animation\"` -- produces cartoon-adjacent results\n- `\"bold outlines, flat colors\"` -- effective as supporting descriptors\n- `\"Cartoon Network animation style\"` -- likely recognized\n- `\"retro cartoon, 1960s animation\"` -- era-specific triggers\n\n**LOW reliability / UNKNOWN:**\n- `\"cartoon\"` as a standalone keyword -- too vague, may produce any of the sub-registers or a generic hybrid\n- `\"UPA style\"` -- too obscure for most training datasets\n- `\"Genndy Tartakovsky style\"` -- individual animator names are unreliable triggers\n\n### 4B. Common Failure Modes\n\n**Style Drift to 3D:**\nAI video models are biased toward photorealism and 3D rendering because their training data is dominated by live-action and 3D-rendered content. A prompt for \"cartoon\" may produce 3D-rendered characters with cartoon proportions (the \"Pixar trap\") rather than genuinely flat 2D.\n\n**Mitigation:** Use supporting descriptors that are incompatible with 3D: `\"flat colors, no gradients, no 3D rendering, hand-drawn look, 2D animation\"`. More effective: provide a 2D cartoon reference image and use image-to-video workflow.\n\n**Style Drift to Anime:**\n\"Animated\" and \"cartoon\" can drift toward anime aesthetics, especially for characters with large eyes or dynamic action poses, because anime is heavily represented in AI training data.\n\n**Mitigation:** Specify `\"Western cartoon style\"` or `\"American animation\"` and describe features that distinguish from anime: `\"round shapes, thick outlines, flat cel colors, no speed lines, no sparkle effects\"`.\n\n**Inconsistency Within a Single Generation:**\n2D cartoon styles can show frame-to-frame inconsistency in line weight, character proportions, and color. Flat-shaded styles are particularly prone to \"drift\" where shading creeps in or outlines thin/thicken across frames.\n\n**Mitigation:** WAN 2.2 is reportedly better than Kling for maintaining flat-shaded style consistency. Using reference images anchors the style more reliably than text-only prompting. Short clips (4-8 seconds) maintain consistency better than long generations.\n\n**\"Style Soup\":**\nToo many style descriptors cause the model to average them unpredictably. `\"cartoon, anime, hand-drawn, cel-shaded, watercolor, vintage\"` will not produce any recognizable style.\n\n**Mitigation:** Pick ONE primary style descriptor. Add 2-3 supporting visual details maximum. Never mix competing registers.\n\n### 4C. The Reference Image Strategy\n\nThis is the most reliable approach for cartoon-style AI video generation:\n\n1. **Generate a still image** in the exact cartoon style you want (using Midjourney, DALL-E, Gemini, etc.)\n2. **Feed the image to Veo 3.1** as a reference with a motion prompt\n3. **The model preserves the visual style** of the input image while adding motion\n\nThis bypasses most text-to-style translation problems. The image IS the style specification.\n\n**Applicability: HIGH / VERIFIED** -- Multiple sources confirm Veo 3.1's strong style preservation from reference images. The Replicate blog specifically notes that \"from cartoon aesthetics to photographic color grading, the model maintains the visual characteristics.\"\n\n### 4D. Does \"Cartoon\" Work as a Keyword?\n\n**Short answer: Not reliably as a standalone.**\n\n\"Cartoon\" is too broad. It encompasses everything from rubber hose to Invincible to Kurzgesagt. The model will select from its training distribution, which likely over-represents modern 3D-influenced cartoon styles.\n\n**Better alternatives ranked by specificity:**\n1. Show/studio name + era: `\"Hanna-Barbera 1960s cartoon style\"` (most specific)\n2. Technical descriptor combo: `\"2D hand-drawn animation, thick black outlines, flat cel colors\"` (medium specific)\n3. Era + register: `\"classic theatrical cartoon, Golden Age animation\"` (medium specific)\n4. Generic: `\"cartoon style\"` (least specific, least reliable)\n\n---\n\n## 5. Cartoon Character Expressiveness\n\n### 5A. Exaggerated Facial Expressions\n\nCartoon facial expression operates on a different grammar than other animation registers:\n\n| Expression | Cartoon Convention | Anime Equivalent | Pixar/3D Equivalent |\n|-----------|-------------------|-----------------|-------------------|\n| Shock | Eyes leave sockets, jaw hits floor, body levitates | Blank face, sweat drop, blue lines | Wide eyes, dropped jaw (within anatomy) |\n| Anger | Face turns red, steam from ears, body inflates | Cross-popping vein, sharp teeth | Furrowed brow, clenched fists |\n| Love | Heart-shaped eyes, floating hearts, tongue rolls out | Sparkle eyes, blushing, flower petals | Soft focus, gentle smile |\n| Fear | Ghost leaves body, legs spin in place before running | Blue face, chibi shrink, cold sweat | Trembling, wide eyes |\n| Speed | Legs become spinning wheel, dust cloud trail | Speed lines, impact frames | Motion blur, lean forward |\n\n**Prompt Applicability: MEDIUM / THEORETICAL**\n\nThese conventions are describable: `\"character's eyes pop out in shock, cartoon style exaggerated reaction\"`. Whether AI video models can produce *physical impossibility as expression* (rather than just \"surprised face\") depends on whether they've learned these as conventions rather than errors. Likely works better with a reference image showing the desired expression level.\n\n### 5B. Physical Impossibility as Expression\n\nCore cartoon-exclusive conventions:\n\n- **Squashed flat (Pancaking):** Character compressed to a disc after being hit. Looney Tunes signature. Sometimes includes accordion-body with actual accordion sound.\n- **Stretching:** Character's neck extends when surprised, limbs stretch to reach things\n- **Shattering:** Character breaks into pieces (like glass or pottery) after impact, then reassembles\n- **Inflation/Deflation:** Character inflates with anger or deflates with sadness\n- **Shape transformation:** Character literally becomes the shape of the object that hit them (anvil-shaped after being hit by anvil)\n\nWalt Disney called this \"The Impossible Plausible\" -- animating physical impossibilities that feel *right* within the cartoon world.\n\n**Prompt Applicability: LOW / THEORETICAL**\n\nThese require the AI model to understand \"cartoon physics\" as a coherent rule system where physical impossibility is intentional, not an error. Current AI video models are optimized for physics-plausible motion. Requesting `\"character flattens like a pancake after being hit by a falling anvil and then pops back to normal shape\"` may produce unpredictable results -- the model might attempt it literally (body horror), smoothly (losing the comedy timing), or refuse it entirely.\n\n### 5C. The \"Take\" as Primary Emotional Beat\n\nIn cartoon acting, the \"take\" IS the punchline. It's not just an expression -- it's an entire body event:\n\n1. **Setup:** Normal scene, character unaware\n2. **Trigger:** Character notices the shocking thing\n3. **Beat/Pause:** Brief freeze (the \"pregnant moment\" per Tex Avery)\n4. **Take:** Full-body explosion of reaction -- every part of the anatomy distorts to sell the emotion\n5. **Recovery:** Character snaps back to normal (or a new normal)\n\nThis 5-beat structure is the fundamental unit of cartoon comedy. It's the cartoon equivalent of the shot-reverse-shot in film.\n\n**Prompt Applicability: LOW / THEORETICAL**\n\nDescribing this as a sequence (`\"character notices the spider, freezes for a moment, then does an extreme cartoon reaction with eyes bulging and body jumping into the air\"`) gives the model a narrative structure. But nailing the TIMING (the pause before the take, the snap of the take itself, the hold on the extreme pose) requires frame-level control that text prompts cannot currently provide.\n\n---\n\n## 6. Practical Prompt Templates by Sub-Register\n\n### Template A: Classic Theatrical Cartoon\n```\nA [character description] in classic Golden Age cartoon animation style, \nhand-drawn 2D animation with thick black outlines and flat cel colors, \nexaggerated squash-and-stretch motion, vibrant saturated palette, \npainted watercolor background, [action/scene description]\n```\n\n### Template B: Rubber Hose / 1930s Vintage\n```\nA [character description] in 1930s rubber hose animation style, \npie-cut eyes, white gloves, round shapes, flowing jointless limbs, \nblack and white with film grain, rhythmic bouncing movement, \nsurreal cartoon world, [action/scene description]\n```\n\n### Template C: Hanna-Barbera / Limited Animation\n```\nA [character description] in Hanna-Barbera cartoon style, \n1960s retro TV animation, bold thick outlines, flat solid colors, \ngeometric character design, simplified backgrounds, \n[action/scene description]\n```\n\n### Template D: Modern Cartoon Network\n```\nA [character description] in modern cartoon style, \nthin clean lines, round soft shapes, bright flat colors, \nsimplified expressive character design, digital 2D animation, \n[action/scene description]\n```\n\n### Template E: Flat Design / Motion Graphics\n```\nA [subject/character description] in flat vector animation style, \nbright neon color palette, geometric shapes, clean design, \nno outlines, smooth transitions and morphing motion, \n[action/scene description]\n```\n\n### Template F: Tartakovsky Action Style\n```\nA [character description] in stylized action animation, \nbold silhouettes, limited color palette, dramatic composition, \nstrong contrast, sharp angular design, dynamic posed action, \n[action/scene description]\n```\n\n---\n\n## 7. Key Recommendations for Prompting\n\n### What to Do\n\n1. **Use the reference image workflow.** Generate a still in your target cartoon sub-register first, then use it as input for Veo 3.1. This is the single most reliable method for maintaining cartoon style.\n\n2. **Pick ONE sub-register and describe its specific visual features.** `\"Hanna-Barbera style with thick outlines and flat colors\"` beats `\"cartoon style\"` every time.\n\n3. **Describe motion as outcome, not technique.** Instead of \"squash and stretch,\" say `\"character compresses before jumping and stretches during the leap.\"` The model understands actions better than animation terminology.\n\n4. **Keep style descriptors to 3-5 tokens maximum.** More causes \"style soup.\"\n\n5. **Use era and studio anchors.** `\"1960s Hanna-Barbera\"` or `\"2010s Cartoon Network\"` provides strong visual context.\n\n### What to Avoid\n\n1. **Do not use \"cartoon\" as a standalone style descriptor.** Too vague. Will default to model's most common interpretation (often 3D-influenced).\n\n2. **Do not mix competing registers** in one prompt. `\"anime cartoon Pixar hand-drawn\"` will produce garbage.\n\n3. **Do not expect frame-precise timing control.** Snap-to-pose, held beats, and comedic timing cannot be specified in current text-to-video prompts.\n\n4. **Do not expect physics-breaking cartoon conventions** (wild takes, body shattering, eye-popping) to work reliably without reference images. The model's physics bias works against these.\n\n5. **Do not stack too many descriptors.** `\"thick outlines, flat colors, no gradients, no 3D, no anime, hand-drawn, cel animation, bold, vibrant, retro\"` is too many competing instructions.\n\n---\n\n## 8. Falsifiability and Uncertainty Flags\n\n### What is VERIFIED (from tested AI outputs or official documentation):\n- Veo 3.1 preserves cartoon visual style well from reference images (Replicate, Google official docs)\n- `\"cel-shaded animation\"` is a recognized and effective style keyword (multiple prompt guides, community reports)\n- Hanna-Barbera and Kurzgesagt produce recognizable results in image generation models (Midlibrary)\n- Too many style descriptors cause unpredictable averaging/\"style soup\" (multiple community reports)\n- WAN 2.2 maintains flat-shaded style better than Kling (community report from Apatero)\n\n### What is THEORETICAL (from animation principles, untested in AI):\n- Most sub-register-specific prompt templates (no published A/B tests for Veo specifically)\n- Whether cartoon motion principles (snap-to-pose, wild takes, smear frames) can be triggered via text prompts\n- Whether era+studio name anchoring (`\"1960s Hanna-Barbera\"`) outperforms technical descriptors (`\"thick outlines, flat colors\"`) in video models\n- Whether negative style prompts (`\"no 3D, no anime\"`) effectively prevent style drift in video generation\n- Whether named show references (`\"Adventure Time style\"`) produce closer matches than generic descriptions\n\n### What would disprove core assumptions:\n- If Veo 3.1 treats all cartoon sub-registers identically regardless of prompt specificity, the taxonomy is irrelevant for prompting\n- If reference images DON'T prevent style drift in video generation, the two-step workflow falls apart\n- If AI video models CAN produce physics-breaking cartoon conventions (wild takes, body shattering) from text alone, the \"physics bias\" limitation is wrong\n\n### Shared assumption across all sources:\nAll community guides assume that the vocabulary of visual features (outlines, flat colors, etc.) maps predictably to model output. This assumption breaks if the model's internal representation of \"cartoon\" is not decomposed along the same visual axes humans use.\n\n---\n\n## Sources\n\n- [Types of Cartoon Styles - Pixune](https://pixune.com/blog/types-of-cartoon-styles/)\n- [Veo Prompt Guide -
